poteuano studiare di giorno, studiauano di notte, non faceuano conto di fatica
di veggiare, fusse cosi hoggi, che il mondo anderebbe in altro modo.

Veramente voi dite il vero, ma di gratia ditemi un poco il vostro parere circa
Diligentia, and che cosa e, perch io ho sentito dire, che e vna cosa molto
profitcuole a chila praticha.

Well met my Sir, I am returned unto you, to desire you, that you will tell me
somewhat this morning, you have so well pleased me in talking of Fortune, that I
am returned to you again, to hear you.

Certis now you mock me.

I do not so certis, believe me.

And what will you that I reason of? I know not almost what. to speak of, I
would that I were in my country.

Then you are not an English man?

No sir. I am an Italian.

How long have you been here in this Realm?

I have been here about a year.

How have you done to learn to speak English so soon?

I have learned English by reading.

May a man learn a language so soon, by reading?

Yea sir, a man may learn it.

Certis I would not have thought it: what think you of this English tongue, tell
me, I pray you?

It is a language that will do you good in England, but pass Dover, it is
worth nothing.

Is it not used then in other cou^treyes?

No sir, with whom will you that they speak?

With English merchants.

English marchantes, when they are out of England, it likes them not, and they
do not speak it.

But yet what think you of the speech, is it gallant and gentle, or else
contrary?

Certis if you will believe me, it does not like me at al, because it is a
language confused, bepeesed with many tongues: it takes many words of the
latin, and mo from the French, and mo from the Italian, and many mo from the
Duitch, some also from the Greek, and from the Britain, so that if every
language had his own words again, there would but a few remain for
English men, and yet every day they add.

How is this thing possible?

It is true, and very true.

Certis I would never have thought it.

Make the experience of it, take a book and read, but mark well, and you
shall not read four words together of true English.

And what think you of this country?

The country likes me very well

What think you of the people? tell me your opinion?

The people are here, as they are in other places, there are good and bad.

What think you of the manners of English men? tell me of courtesy.

I will tell you, some are well manered, but many ill.

Toward whom are they ill manered?

Toward Strangers: and few of these English men delight to have their children
learn diverse languages, which thing displeases me. When I arrived first in
London, I could not speak English, and I met above five hundred persons,
before I could find one, that could tell me in Italian, or French, where the
Post dwelt.

And what would you have them do? learn languages:

Yea sir, and bring up their children well, and have them taught to read,
write, and speak diverse languages, and not do, as many of these English
Gentlemen do, that I know.

And what do they?

I see certain Gentlemen rather lownes, to tell the truth, that begin to learn
to spoke Italian, French, and Spanish, and when they have learned two words of
Spanish, three words of French, and four words of Italian, they think they
have yenough, they will study mo more.

Think you it be possible, that an English man may learn the Italian tongue,
and that an Italian may learn the Enghish.

Yea sir, that it is possible.

In what time think you?

Thereafter as he plyeth it, I have known the^ that have learned Italian in
three months.

And what profit comes to those that can read, write, and speak many
languages? I know not.

How? the profit that comes thereof is very great.

I find it not so: I read, write, and speak, three or four tongues, and yet
I find no profit by it.

I will say, as Alfonsus king of Aragon said, being sick.

And what did he say?

Alfonsus king of Aragon, being sick, in Capua, gave him self to read the
works of Titus Linius, and of Quintus Curtius: and being healed of his
sickness, he was wont to say, that when he was sick, he never found better
medicine, then reading Quintus Curtius: being sad, he found no better music,
then in reading of Titus Liuius: Augustus Caesar would never be without Virgil
in his hand: Alexander the Great, could never take rest, unless he had
Homers Iliades before his eyes: Pompey held him self happy, when he had Cicero
in his bosom: glad was Scipio, when he read Enneus: gioconde was the Emperor
Gratian, when he read the Posies of Ausonius. Verily, I must say with Alfonsus,
that readyng is the best medicine for a sick man, the best music for a sad
man, the best mirth for a heavy man, the best counsel for a desperate man, the
best comfort for one afflicted, the best consolation for a diseased man, the
best learning for an ignorant man, the best wisdom for a fool, the best
exhortations, and the best admonitions that are for young men. By readyng, many
things are learned, who will have good counsel, let him read, who will see, and
hear strange things, let him read: by reding, we have good forwarning, by
reading, we learn to know the good from the bad, virtue from vice, and as the
be takes from one herb, gum, from another wax, and from an other honey, so by
reading diverse books, diverse things are learned: by reading Retnorik we learn
to persuade, by reading Logik, we learn to know the right from the wrong, by
reading Philosoyhie, we learn the secrets of nature, by reading D^uinitie, we
learn how a man should govern him self, to attain life everlasting, by
reading histories, you may see how so many and so many kingdo^s have been
governed: and as Cicero says, in praise of history, history, says he, is the
testimony of Time, the light of verity, the life of memory, the guide of time,
the messenger of antiquity. Finally, by reading histories, we see how long time
mighty Emperoures, great kings, famous commonweals, and peopled cities have
flourished, we see how time flees away, and if it were not for Writers that
have been, al these things were not known as they are: and if it were not for
historiographers, and writers that have been, how should so many things be
known? And as Salustius says, when he exhorts every one to read, and to
praise Writers, he says, that the best consolation for one afflicted, is
reading, the best liberty for a prisoner, and the best comfort that is for a
melancholique man, is reading. He says moreover of writers, that those that
did any famous deeds, deserved not so great fame in dooyng them, as did the
Writers in writing them in losty style: how should Alexander the great be so
famous, if Q. Curtius had not written of him? what were Ulysses and Achilles,
if Homer had not ben borne? what were Alcibiades, if Xenophon had not exalted
him? what Cirus, if Chilus had not made mention of him in his works? what
Pirrus king of Albania, if Hermicles had not compiled a Chronicle of him? what
had the great Africa, been if the Decades of Titus Liuius had not ben? what
Traiane, if Plutarch had not ben so frindly unto him? what were Nerua, and
Antonius Pius, if Phocion the Greek had not written of them? How should we
know of the great courage of Julius Caesar, and of the greatness of Pompey, if
Lucanus had not written of the^? who should know the lives of the twelve
Cesars, if Suetonius Tranquillus had not made a book of the^? what should we
know of the antiquities of the Hebrews, if our well willing Joseph had not left
memory of them? who should know the coming of the Longobadies into Italy, if
Paulus Diaconus had not written of it? How should we know the coming and
progress of the Goths into Spain, if the curious Rodericus had not spoken of
them? who should know the paths of Aeneas, if Virgil had not composed of him?
how should the moral documents, with so many fine posies, with the louyngs and
transformations of the gods be known, if our learned Ovid had not taken such
a great labor upon him? how should the life of Marcus Aurelius be so manifest?
and how he was husband to Faustina, father to Comodus, brother to Annius Verus,
son in law to Antonius Pius, friend to Polion, uncle to Egesippus, and the
seventeen Emperor of Rome, if Erodianus had not loved him? Giulius Capitolinus
honored him, and Eutropius written of him? who should know the courtesies of
Leo to Rugiero? the loves of Rugiero and Bradamante? the wars of Agrama^te and
Charles, the co^bats of Rodomonte and Mandricardo? the sightinges of Ferau and
Rinaldo, the great enterprises of Guido^ Seluaggio? the boldness of Marfila?
the amorous toys of Orlando and Angelica, if that our gentle, learned,
gallant, and worthy Poet Lodonico Ariosto had not compyled a book of it:
Finally, how should we know the delivery of the virgin the birth of Jesus
Christ our Saueour, his passion under Pontius, how he suffered death upon the
Cross, how he was buried, how he rose the third day, how he sits on the
right hand of God the father almighty, and how he shall come from thence to
judge the quick and dead? who should know that he had twelve disciples? and
how one of them betrayed him? which were the works and acts of the Apostles?
what was the death of S. John Baptist, of S. Paul, of S. Peter, and of the
traitor Judas? how should we know in what consists al our salvation? how
God has created us, Jesus Christ bought us, and the holy ghost sanctified us?
how should these things be known, if the four Evangelists had not written so
much? Salustius says, the memory of Writers shall last for ever: for neither
time can consume, neither fortune destroy their fame. How should we know, if
it were not for Writers and Historiographers, how Licurgus was he that gave
Laws to the Lacedaemonians? How Numa Pompilius honored the Temples? how
Marcus Marcellus wept at the fortune of those that he had overcome? how Julius
Caesar pardoned his enemies? how Octauianus was beloved of his people? how
Seuerus did help all men? how Hector Troianus was courageous in Martial and
wa^like affairs? How Hercules used his forces? how Pirrhus king of Albania was
inuentor of so many ingenious things? how Marcus Mar cellus suffered so many
torments? how Titus was father to the Orfanes? how Traian framed so many
buildings, and was counted the good? Adrian the wise, Antonius the pitisul? how
Semiraw is sinned fleshly with her own son, and afterward was slain by him?
how Tarquinius forced Lucretia? how Brutus slew Caesar? how Silla shed so
much blood? how Catilina played the tirant over his country? how Iugurth slew
his own brethren? how Caligula violated his sisters? how Nero slew his
mother? how Heliogaballus robbed the Temples? how Domitianus caused his own
people to be slain of strangers, while he was killyng of Flies? how should we
know, if it were not for writers, how desire of fame moved Minos to raise so
many wars? and Semiramis his wise to build so many edifices? Ulysses the
Grecia^ to sail so many seas? Alexander the great to go through so many
countries? Hercules the Theban to set his pyllers, and where he set them? Caius
Caesar the Roman, to make fifty and two battles? Hannibal the Carthaginia^, to
make such sharp wars against the Romans? Pirrhus K. of Albania, to come into
Italy? Atillas king of the Hunnes, to war against al Europe? Al these things
we do know, thanks be unto Writers, that have loved us, but we love not them.
How should we know, if it had not ben for Writers, how Eve was the first
sinner? Cain the first mutherer? Abel the first that dyed? Lamech the first
that had two wives? Enoc the first that founded City? Tubalchaim the first
Musition? Noe the first that sailed? Nembroth the first Tyrant? Melchisedech
the first Priest? Anrafel the first king in the world? Moses the first
Duke? Julius Caesar the first Emperor: Belon the first that found Wars?
Assiria the first Monarchy? Cadorlaomor the first that found the use of
weapons? Romulus the first king of the Romans? Egineta the first that caused
money to be made? Darius the first that caused tribute to be paid? the Scithians
the first that found bows and arowes: Vulcan the first that found fire
Anacharses that found belows to blow it? Hipocrates the first Physician?
Esculapius the first Surgeon? Aristeus the first that found honey? the Lidians
the first to dye wool? those of Egypt found the use of Flax: those of Frigia,
to sow with needles: those of Hetruria, the first to weaue: Dionifius the
first that found victories and trivmphes: Moses the first that found letters
among the Hebrews: Fidias the first Painter. Of al these things should we be
ignorant, if Writers had not ben. Thanks be unto the^, it is knowoen how
Jerusalem was the head city of al Palestina: Rome the head city of Italy, and
was called the head of the world: but it is not so, Car thage the head city of
Africa: Numantia of Spain: Argentina of Germany: Babylon of Chaldea: Thebes of
Egypt: Athens of Greece: Tirus of Fenicia: Cesarea of Cappadocia: Bizancio of
Thrace. Now are London of England, Paris of France, Venice of Italy, Augusta
of Germanic, Constantinople of Turchie, Vienna of Austrige, Prage of Bohemia,
Buda of Hungaria, Lisbona of Portugal, Siuiglia of Spain, Edenborough of
Scotland, Duuelin of Irelande, Andwerpe of the low countries, Vrbino of
Piemount, Marocco of Barbary, if it had not been for Historiographers? how
should we know what the ambition of Caesar was? the dru^kennesse of Tiberius?
the pride of Calicula? the cruelty of Nero? the vicious life of Heliogabalus?
the pity of Traian? the envy of Alexander? the beauty of Narcissus? the
wisdom of Solomon? the forces of Hercules, and Hector? the avarice of Cirus?
the liberality of Pompey? the frindship between Damon and Pithias? the
Philosophy of Aristotle? the learning of Cato? the eloquence of Tullie? the
riches of Cressus? the patience of Adrastus? the swiftness of Atalanta? the
age of Nestor? the policy of Ulysses? the obedience of Abraham? the
covetousness of Midas the craft of Hannibal? the dissembling of Zopirus? the
flattery of Aristippus? the memory of Mithridates? and the constancy of
Penelope? None of these things should so be in the mouth of every man, if
Writers had not ben, which are worthy to be honored. If it were not for
Writers, there should be no fear of God, no Law, nor order: every one would
live after his own manner. By reading we see the diversities of things, the
goodness of one sort, and the badnes of the other, we learn to know the good
from the bad. For as the Poet says, There is no light without darkness, no
virtue without vice, no shadow without a body, no commodity without a
discommoditie. By reading we learn to be eloquent, and being eloquent, many
and innumerable be the commodities that ensue of it. Eloquence has force to
make the coward courageous the tirant courteous and merciful: Eloquence
persuades the good, disswadeth the bad, comforts the afflicted, banisheth
fear from the fearful, pacifieth the insolent: and, as Cicero says,
vanquisheth cities, kingdo^es, and castles with her force. Eloquence in
adversity, is a solace: in prosperity, an ornament: in youth, laudable: in
age, delectable: in all estates profitable: it does good to all men, it
hurts none. Pirrhus that great king of Albania was wont to say, that he
won more cities, overcame more realms with the eloquence of Cineas, then he
did with his great armies. Behold, if eloquence be of great force, and how can
we learn it without letters? It is not possible. Tell me of courtesy, if it
had not been for so many and so many Doctors, Philosophers, and writers, that
have labored so, day and night, in studying for to leave memory of them, and to
give us to learn, how should we know what the worshipping of God is? we
should be without understanding, as brute beasts: we should never think on
God, to serve him: nor of his glory, to obey him: nor on the poor, to succor
them: nor on our life, to amend it, neither on our consciences, to brighten
them, but with the help of books we learn to setue God, we learn to obey
his glory, we learn to succor the poor, (but few do it,) we learn to
amend our lives, and to brighten our consciences, through the doctrine of
books, we learn to know, how God, of earth has created us unto his
likeness, how Jesus Christ has redeemed us with his precious blood, shed
for our sins, how the holy ghost has glorified us, how God the father, God
the son, and God the holy Ghost, are three in one, and one in three, and
that there is none other means, or way, for to obtain life everlasting, then
by him three, and them one: unto the which life everlasting, God for his grace
conduct us. Amen.

Certis great account have we to make of ancient Writers, yet will I not
praise them al, for I know that there are many, yea very many that write more
then they ought. for as every thing is corupted now adays, even so is writin,
corrupted, (...) any write things that are not to be written much less to be
read and well less to be licenced, but of that I leave the care unto those that
have authority to correct and amend every thing. And so, for so much as I
have said, and am to say, Readers may conjecture, how much we owe to
Historiographers, who in my mind, left of them selves immortal glory, for that
as they wrote, as those Princes were glorious, for that which they did, the
studious Writers have little esteemed the great travails they have suffered, in
readyng, studying, and writing. Notwithstanding, knowing how always there
were, are, and I believe, will be certain groutheaded, ill manered, and to say
better, envious, that when Writers pass through labor, they go abroad
solasing: when Writers watch, they sleep: when Writers fast, they are a
banquettyng, and a feastyng: when that the Writers are tossyng and turnyng
their books, they go wallowing in vices: and yet for all this, they will set
the^ selves to judge, to mar^e, and to condemn other men's doctrine, and even
if they had the authority that Plato had in Greece, that Solomon had among the
Hebrews, that Licurgus among the Lacedaemonians, Premetheus among the Egyptians,
Apolonius among the Indians, and Secu^dus among the Assyrians, or else the
cloquence that Cicero had in Rome Few you shall find, I be leave none, that be
now adays in the Latin tongue diligent, in the Greek expert, in the Hebrew
learned, with the Spanish adorned, upon the Italian language well grounded, and
in the French curious, or else in tossing of books desirous, and if any do it,
he is mocked of many, iested at of every body, envied of al, imitated of few,
loved of less, sought of none. You shall find certain doltes, better fed the^
taught, certain Antony nownowes, who before they have seen a book, before they
know what it is, and whereof it treates, will be so bold as to defame it. You
shall see certain men, not men, but rather beasts, (I am almost minded to name
them) who are so large in speaking, and so straight in studying, that with out
blushyng they will dare to say, that there is no book but they have readde, or
else seen: behold what presumption is this, every one is given to condemn
books, few to commend the^. You shall see three or four in a company, and one
will take a book in his hand, and begin to read two or three-words, and one
will say, he is too coy: another will say, he speaks out of purpose: one will
say, he is too dark: an other will say, it is not good vulgar: another will say,
al is feigned: another will say, he is to curious: another, he is crafty, so
that, to speak briefly, the matter rests as suspected, and the Author goes
not altogether unspotted. There are certain other shameless, that without
shame will be so hardy, as to say, that there rests nothing to see, but they
have seen it: they will vaunt themselves, that there is no one thing to
discover, that they have not discovered, affyrming that there is nothing to be
read, that they have not read they will swear that there is nothing to be
written, that they have not written: some other will assure the^ selves, that
there is nothing to be known that the^ know not. (Ha crafty people, ha shameless
nation) who art cause that many and many that would write, leave of, and al
through such envious men. Tell me of courtesy, the greatest Princes that have been have they not taken delight in Writers and Philosophers? Plutarch, was not
he master unto Traian? Alexander, had not he Homer for his friend? and
Aristotle for his master, Ha noble sayeing of Philip, that great king of
Macedonia, and father to Alexander the great, when as he said, that he thanked
his gods that his son Alexander was borne, whilst Aristotle lived, and
gave him, him to be his Tutour: that great king Darius, had not he for his
familiare, Plotinus? Augustus had Pistus, Pompey had Plautus, Titus had
Plinius, Adrianus had Secundus, Antonius had Apolonius, Theodosius had
Claudius, S^ncrus had Fabatus: Philosophers in the old time had such authority
in princes houses, that princes sons did acknowledge them for Fathers, and
their Fathers for Masters. (Few now adays do imitate them, and less do
compare with the^) how much good Philosophy does unto princes and lords, let
us learn of Marcus Aurelius, who was exalted unto the Empire, not for his
riches, but for his learning, not for his high blood, but for his Philosophy,
not because he did inherit it, but because he had deserved it by his good
works. He was a Philosopher very sage, a prince very mighty, an Emperor much
feared, of a right judgment in all justice, grateful unto all his friends,
pacient in his trauayles, how well he could dissemble with his enemies, how
severe against tyrants, how peaceable with the peaceable, how frindly to the
wise, how he did endeavor himself to imitate the simple, how fortunate in his
wars, how bountiful in Peace: he was temperate in eating, moderate in
spending, grateful to them that served him, pacient to such as offended him,
dilligent to mainteyn peace, faithful in keeping truces, he was a lord of a deep
understanding, learned in the Greek and Latin tongue, and beside all this,
how hauty in his words, how profound in his Sentences. Verily you see his
works imitated of few, but forsaken of many. Oh how much have Philosophers
been esteemed in old time, so many and so many histories make mention of it.
You see how the Philosopher Brusillus was esteemed of all the Senate of Rome?
Oh how much were the seven Sages of Greece esteemed? you may read it: how much
Anatharsus was esteemed of Cressus, it is manifest that he sent his Ambassadors
from Lidia to Athens with wany treasures, to ask for him, and pray him he would
come see him, promising him the government of al his realm and counted himself
deformed and unfortunate, because he had no Philosophers to keep him company,
and to counsel him. Falaris that great tyrant would delight in learned me^,
and in Philosophy, and would never be without. Oh golden age, when learning
was sought for far and near: when wit was exercised, and policy practiced,
and virtue honored. Well did Tullie say the truth, when as he said, O
Philosophy, the searcher of al good virtues, and the expeller of all vices,
that common weal did account it self happy, that Realm blessed, that City
in peace, that had good Philosophers in it, for to exalt virtue, and to
suppress vice, to reward the good, and punish the bad, to esteem the learned,
and neglect the ignorant. Wherefore my opinion is, that Writers be made account
of, and that their good works be esteemed: but it is not so, well may we hope
for it, but not look for it.

Verily you have made me here a long Sermon, that I believe you should be weery.

Yea verily I am weery, and the more I speak of them, the more I may ^^e more
I praise them, the more they merit to be praised. Now ad^^es few give
themselves to read, and less to study: verily it greueth me to think upon it.

I believe that many do it for to spare money.

So they do certain.

I would there were such a Law, that if one should bring up his children,
without teaching them somthyng, and especially to read, write, and speak
diverse la^guages, that he should be beheaded, or else punished grievously.

Yea, but we may rather hope for such a thing; then look for it. Oh God, when
I consider the grievous Laws that have been against those that were idle in
the old time. O triumphing Rome, when every one was bound to learn something,
and if by chance any one were found idle, he was grievously punished, as well
his parents, as he: then were there not so many robberies, stealynges,
filchings, and murdrings. And whence come these things, but only because youth
is so delicatly brought up, that when their fathers and mothers approach unto
death, they can do nothing, if they have any thing, they spend it quickly, if
they have nothing, they go a stealing, and become high way thieves. What is
their end? they prove if Hemp be strong, as there is daily experience of it,
and al these things might be shunned, if it would please their fathers to
think thereupon when they are young, and not pamper them up so proudly, nor
bring the^ up so idly. What a shame is it, that you shall see an English man
come in company of strangers, who can neither speak, nor understand with
them, but stands as one mute, and so is he mocked of them, and despised of al,
and none will make account of him? What a shame is that? what a reproach to his
parents? what a loss to him? and what hearts grief to think thereon? Learning
and wisdo^e, let fortune do what she please that never is lost, waters can not
mar it, nor fire burn it if it prevail thee not in one place, it will
prevail thee in an other: no tempest can consume Science. Oh how well was it
said of the Poet, when one demanded of him, where all his treasures were, he
aunsweared, In my bosom. Learning is heavy, and yet it waieth not, it is
great, and takes up no great place, it is fair, and yet few seek it,
profitable, and yet is forsaken of many: she is sweet, but yet few will taste
of her: brave Palaces, are they not subject unto tempest? beauty of body unto
sickness? riches unto Fortune? Who has money, can buy no learning lightly, and
who has learning, finds palaces and riches, fame, glory, reputarion, and
money, come where he will. The ancient Philosophers did understand it aright,
for if they could not study by day, they studied by night, they counted it no
labor to wake, were it so now adays, the world would go after an other manner.

Verily you say true, but tell me a little of courtesy, your judgment, of
Diligence, and what it is, for I have heard say, that it is a thing very
profitable, to the^ that practice it.

Ragionamenti sopra Diligentia, Humanita Clementia, tempera^tia, and Sobrieta,

IO vi diro il me glio che io posso, Diligentia si puo chiamar vna de le
principal figlie di virtu, perch come dice il Filosofo, la piu gran virtu che
sia, si e di abandodonar otio, and la piu grande comendatione che l'huomo puo
hauere, si e di esser diligen e in cercar di ottener bone cose: diligentia e
habile di far la piu difficile cosa facile, la piu cruda cosa matura, le piu
strane cose familiare, la piu dura cosa molle: con diligentia, tanti e tanti
Filosofi diuennero cosi famosi. Fra gli antichi si dice, che inanzi che
Industria e Diligentia fusse conosciuta, non si sap^a che cosa fusse ordine, ma
quando che si comincio a studiar, e cercar a conoscere il been dal male, dico,
che qua^do che si commncio a praticar astutia, che si metteua cose in ordine,
che ragione comincio a reggere, quando che si comincio a cercar dotrina,
esercitar virtu, al'hora venne vna eta nuoua, and e ben vero quello che dice
Tullio, O Filosofia, la vera fontana di ogni virtu, and la persettione di
intendimento, and ben felice si puo dir quel regno, dove che il prencipe cerca
con diligentia di esaltar virtu, di supressar vitio, di remunerar i boni, di
punir i cat^iui, di honorar i dotti, di odiar i ignoranti: dove che non si
porta rispetto a nessuno, ne a nobile, ne a ignobile, ma che ogni cosa si regge
secondo la verita, la qual cosa si fa rare volte hoggi di.

Certo io credo che voi siate profeta, perch dite il vero, ma se non vi
dispiace, che io vi dia tanto fastidio, di chiarir^ mi che cosa sia Humanita et
Clementia, perch io vedo che sono si rado praticate fra gli huomini hoggi di
che credo che non siano buone e profiteuole.

Io vi diro il piu broue che io posso, ma la mia capacita e cosi cattiua, che
no^ posso adempir il vostro desiderio, ma acetiate la mia bona volenta, cosi
facendo, fate da fedel amico, perch io non sono dott^.

Io per sempre acetto la sua bona volonta mostrata verso di me, certo.

A dirui la verita, si come superbia opressa amore, prouoca sdegno, genera
malitia, confunde giustitia, e corompe gran stati, cosi Humanita e Clementia
genera vna certa afetione, aumenta amicitia, mantiene amore, suporta equita:
finalmente, preserua ogni gran stato: Perch come dice Alessandro Seuero, non
ci e niente che facci un prencipe amato da i suoi sugietti, che humanita,
nessuna cosa aquista honor a un Nobile piu presto che l, esser clemente, niente

esser clemente, i suoi Consiglieri humani, i suoi magistrati cortesi and
amoreuoli: piu honor aquistara un prencipe a dire, questa cosa posso fare, che
dire, questa cosa voglio fare, e far been per male, che agiungere male sopra
male. Perch che piu nobil virtu puo esser in un prencipe, che l'esser
clemente, pronto in perdonar, lento in castigar. Che eccelentia e in un Nobile?
che ornamento in un Gentilhuomo? che comendatione in ciascaduno, l'esser
clemente? acresce fama, aquista honor, guadagna vittorie. Al contrario, se
l'huomo e superbo, altiero, e tiranno, lui aquista infamia, lui aquista odio da
molti, amor da pochi, e molte volte perde il stato: cosi a dirui la mia
opinione, innumerabile sono le comodita che vengono a l'huomo essendo humano,
clemente, e liberale. Ma al contrario, essendo auaro, crudele, e superbo, lui e
sottoposto a la varieta di Fortuna: lui e sottoposto a molti pericoli e danni,
e anche a morte.

Certo voi dite il vero, ma certo noi Inglesi siamo obligati a render infinite
gratie a Dio, che ci ha concesso si bona Regina, cosi humana, cosi clemente,
cosiliberale, and cosi misericordiosa, che poche se ne trouano sue pari.

Certissimamente voi dite il vero, ma noi siamo cosi ingrati verso Dio per la
sua bonta, in concedercela, and verso lei mamarci, verso noi stessi in non
conoscerci noi stessi, and si^mo cosi corotti da ingratitudine.

Orsu, se vi piace, ditemi in poco, in tree o quatro parole, che cosa sia
Sobrieta and Temperantia, and anche se e cosa cosi degna da esser abracciata,
come era all' anticha.

Veramente io vi diro il mio parere, Temperantia e vna virtu, che non si puo
laudar a bastanza come merita, ma il meglio che so, velo diro in quatro parole,
Sobrieta and Temperantia sono virtu, che non sono tanto stimate adesso come
sono state altri tempi, and pochi tengono le Regole di Anarchase Filosofo, che
era molto dotto.

Che Regole sono quelle?

Il Filososo persuade i prencipi a esser temperati in vita, sobrij in parlar, e
astenersi da mangiar molto, per che in vincer apetito, and concupiscentia, and
supressar piacere, e vna grande vittoria. Colui e valente vittore, famoso
Conquistore, e potente prencipe, che sa vincer se medesimo, un gran numero di
bone virtu acompagnano Sobrieta and Temperanza, come abstinentia, continentia,
chastita, and sapientia: and al contrario, un mondo de vitij ne seguono, come
ebrieta, lusuria, and altre furfantarie. La compagnia di ebrij, e piu odiosa,
che profitteuole: piu dangerosa, che sana, piu sporcha, che amicheuole: la vita
del vno e famosa, la vita del'laltro infamosa: la temperanza e virtu del'uno e
comendeuole, la insatieta and malitia del altro e da esser condannata, el ci
hisogna considerar che noi mangiamo per viuer, and non viuiamo per mangiare,
come fanno molti. Tree Leggi erano in Thebe, che erano in grande estimatione: La
prima di ad'orar i lor Deij: la seconda, di honorar i suoi parenti: la terza,
di astener da mangiar troppo, e pascere cosi ingordisiamente, come si fa hoggi.
O te^perata vita, qua^do temperantia era osseruata: O mondo d'orato, quando che
ne vino, ne banchetti erano conosciuti, al'ho ra chastita era conosciuta nel
Tempio di Vesta. Al'hora gli Imperatori frequentauano la Capella di Jupiter,
al'hora concupi scentia non ardiua venir ne la Corte de Cesare, al'hora
abstinentia caminaua per il mercato in ogni Citta, al'hora il mondo era chasto,
al'hora il mondo triumfaua, ma adesso ogni cosa va ala rouersa.

Certo e vna cosa lamenteuole, a considerar il stato di questo mondo.

I Will tell you as well as I can, Diligence may be called one of the principal
daughters of Virtue for even as the Philosopher says, the greatest virtue that
is, is to flee idleness, and the greatest commendation that any man can have,
is to be diligent in seeking to obtain good things: Diligence is able to make
the vneasyest thing, easy: the rawest thing ripe, the straungest things,
familiar: the hardest thing, soft: with Diligence, so many, and so many
Philosophers became so famous. It is said, that in the former age, before
Industry and Diligence were known, none knew what order was, but when that
they began to study, and to seek to know the good from the bad, I say, when
that they began to practice polici, that they did put things in order, and
that reason began to rule, when they began to seek out learning, and
exercise virtue, then came a new age: and it is true that Tulli^ says, O
Philosophy, the true fountain of all virtues, and the perfection of
understanding, and well may that Realm be called happy, where the prince seeks
with diligence to exalt virtue, and supresse vice, to reward the good, and
punish the wicked, to honor the learned, and hate the ignorant: where no
respect is borne no more to one then to another, either to the noble, or to the
unnoble, but that every thing is ruled after the truth, the which is seldom
seen now adays.

Certis I believe that you are a Prophet, for you say true, but if it do not
displease you, that I trouble you so much, as to tell me what thing Humanity
and Clemency is, for I see that they are so seldom used among men now a
days, that I believe they are not good, nor profitable.

I will tell you as briefly as I can, but my capacity is so bad, that I fear
me I can not very well fulfill your desire, but accept my good will, and so
doing, you shall do like a faithful friend, for I am not very well learned.

Certis I always accept your good will, always showed toward me.

To tell you the truth, eue^ as pride oppresses love, provoke disdain,
ingendreth malice, co^foundeth justice, and corrupts great states, so
humanity and cleme^cie engenders a certain affection, augme^teth frind ship,
mainteineth love, supporteth equity: finally it preserves any great estate.
For even as Alexander seuerus says, there is nothing that makes a prince to
be beloved of his subjects, then humanity, nothing sooner gets honor unto
a Noble man, then to be clement, nothing gendreth love, mainteyneth concord,
peace, and quietness among the subjects, the^ a prince to be clement, his
Cou^sellers humane, his Magistrates courteous and loving: more honor shall a
Prince purchase, to say, this thing I can do, then to say, this thing I will
do, and do good against ill, then to add evil upon evil. For what more
noble virtue can be in a Prince, then to be clement, ready to forgive, and
slow to punish? What an excellency in a Noblema^? what an ornament in a
Gentleman? what commendation in all men, is it to be clement? it increases
fame, purchases honor, and gets victories. Contrariwise, if a man be
proud, hauty, and a tyrant, he purchases infamy, gets hate of many, love
of none, and many times he loses his estate: and so, to tell you my opinion,
innumerable are the commodities that come unto man, to be humane, clement, and
liberal. But contrariwise, if he be covetous, cruel, and proud, he is subject
unto the variety of Fortune: he is subject to many perils and dangers, and
also unto death.

Certis you say true, but verily we English me^ are bound to render infinite
thanks to God, for that he has granted us so good a Queen, so humane, so
clement, so liberal, and so mercyful, that few are found her peers.

Undoubtedly you say true, but we are so ungrateful toward God for his
goodness in granting her to us, and toward her in loving us, and toward our
selves, in not knowing our selves, and we are so corrupted of ingratitude.

Well, if it please you to tell me in three or four words, what Sobriety and
Temperance is, and also if it be a thing so worthy to be embraced, as it was
in the old time?

Verily I will tell you my mind, Temperance, is a virtue that can not be praised
so much as it deserves, but as well as I can, I will tell it you in four
words, Sobriety and Temperance, are virtues, that are not so much esteemed
now, as they have been in times tofore, and few hold the rules of Anacharsis
the Philosopher, who was well learned.

What Rules are those?

The Philosopher persuades princes to be te^perate in their life, sober in
speaking, and to ab stain from much eating, to overcome appetite and lust: and
to suppress pleasure, is a great victory. He is a valiant victor, a famous
Conqueror, and a mighty prince, that can vanquish him self, a gteat number of
good virtues do accompany Sobriety and Temperance, as abstinence,
continency, chastity, and wisdom: and contrary, a world of vices do follow,
as drunkenness, lechery, and other villainies. The company of drunkardes is
more odious, then profitable: more dangerous, then healthful: more filthy,
then friendly: the life of the one is famous, the life of the other infamous:
the temperance and virtue of the one is commendable, the insaciety and
wickedness of the other is to be condemned: we must consider, that we do eat
to live, and not live to eat, as many do. Three Laws were in Thebes, that
were in great estimation: The first was, to woorshyp their Gods: the second,
to honor their parents: the third, to absteyne from too much eating, and
feeding so excessiuely, as is used now adays. O temperate life, when
temperance was observed: O golden world, when neither Wine, nor banquettes
were known, then was chastity known in the Temple of Vesta. Then the
Emperors did frequent the Chapel of Jupiter, then Lust durst not come to the
Court of Caesar, then abstinence walked through the markette in every Cittye,
then the world was chaste, then the world did triumph, but now every thing
goes contrary.

Certis it is a lamentable thing, to consider the state of this world.

Ragionamenti sopra Silentio, and Liberalita, and in laud di Eta. Cap. 29.
Reasonynges upon Silence and Liberality and in praise of Age. Chap. 29.
DItemi vi prego, comendate voi Silentio, e Taciturnita, come hanno fatto molti
al tempo vechio?

Silentio e cosa degna da esser comendata da tutti, abracciata da molti, and
accarezata da ogni stato.

Socrate insegnaua a i suoi scolari tree cose: la prima, da esser taciturno nela
lingua: la seconda, vergognoso in viso: la terza, sauio in cuore: perch dice,
La sapientia del pazzo, e ne la sua lingua, and la lingua del sauio e ascosta
nel suo cuore. Demostene dice, Silentio in un Prencipe, e cosa necessaria:
Silentio e vna rara virtu, Silentio e gioia di gran valor: Tu non fai male a
nessuno, tu non tradisci nessuno, tu non inganni nessuno, e pur Silentio e la
piu pesante soma che sia, il piu grieve pensier, un disagio che sempre brucia,
chesempre stimola l'huomo. O qua^to pochi seen trouano di quelli hoggi di, che
sanno tacere fra gli huomini? manco fra le done.

Certo voi dite il vero, pochi se ne trouano.

Ditemi di gratia, che cosa e liberalita, io credo che sia un gran vitio.

Perch dite voi cosi?

Perch si pochi la vsano.

E per quello debbe esser vitio?

Che so io? Io vedo che si pochi vsano Liberalita, che io pensaua che fusse,
come a dir un gran tradimento, perch si vsa cosi rare volte.

Ma non vedete voi, che il mo^do va al contrario, dove che l'huomo doueria vsar
liberalita, lui vsa auaritia e cupidita, Liberalita e vna gran virtu, and per
quello pochi la vsano, e manco la praticano. Liberalita e vna virtu accarezata
da pochi, disprezata da molti, pochi se ne trouano, che in dar sono presti, and
in acettar lenti: pochi vogliono creder a Curtio, quando che lui dice, Che a
posseder assai non e richezze, ma auaritia e cupidita, e al contrario, a non
desiderar niente, ma donar assai, quello e un posseder gran richezze, and esser
liberale. Pochi credo nessuno se netrouera hoggi di, che siano come molti sono
stati in Roma, che erano franchi e liberali verso la piu paate, benefattori de
tutti, e auari verso nessuno, e i richi teniuano casa aperta. Liberalita in un
prencipe, gli aquista amore e feed: a un Nobile, seruitio and bona volonta da
tutti. Liberalita aguista a ogniuno beneficij: ma pochi dicono con Alessandro
Imperatore.

Che cosa dice lui?

Lui dice, che il preucipe doueria sempre essere piu pronto and presto in dare,
che in pigliare.

O nobil detto da un prencipe.

Fu un altro che domando a Alessandro, dove erano tutte le sue richezze, che
lui haueua aquistate in tante guerre che lui haueua fat: lui fece segno verso
i suoi sudditi, and disse, ne li cuori de la la mia gente.

O nobil prencipe.

Dice lui, che piu gran cosa puo desiderar un prencipe da i suoi sugetti, che
feed e verita verso lui? che piu laudabil cosa in un prencipe, che liberalita
and lenita verso i suoi sudditi: la liberalita di un pouero, si e il suo bon
volere. Che piu gran dono puo dar l' huomo, che quello che gli vien dal cuore?
Fu vo Re in Thebe, che fu si liberale mentre visse, che quando mori, non si gli
trouo tanti denari apresso per sepelirlo: non si gli trouo denari in Banchi,
oro in casse, gioie in cofani, richezze nascoste.

De quelli se ne troua pochi. Perch piu che l'huomo ha, piu lui ha da temer la
mutabilidilita di Fortuna. Ogniuno e liberale in parlare, ma pochi franchi in
donare: ogniuno parla contra inuidia e malitia, e pur ci odiamo l'un l'altro.
Noi continualmente esclamiamo contra tirannia, e pur siamo senza misericordia,
noi dispreziamo superbia, e pur siamo senza humanita, noi abhoriamo glotonia
and ebrieta, and pur sempre siamo a banchetti, a feste: noi sempre cridiamo
contra otio, e pur sempre siamo otiosi: noi sempre diciamo male de la lingua
che scandaliza, e pur non sapiamo dir been di nessun^. O Dio, a che termine
siamo, ogniuno ha inuidia al suo prossimo, ogniuno cerca di auanza l'altro.

Io credo che il mondo sia quasi ala fine.

Cosi credo anche io.

Certo signor voi mi hauete compiaciuto in molte cose, ma anchora vi prego a
dirmi qualco sa di Eta.

Bione quel Filo sofo dice, che Eta e il vltimo porto di riposo, il fine di
ogni miseria, la porta di vita, la osseruatrice de tuttii pilgrimaggi, and vna
cosa honoreuole, desiderata da tutti. Fu vno che gli doman do perch lui
laudaua tanto Eta? lui rispose, se been la prima vera e piaceuole, l' Autuno e
fertile, i fiori che crescono nela prima vera ne l' Autuno si maturano, cosi
lui compara l'eta del' huomo ale quatro stagione del'anno, il tempo che lui
cresce, e come la prima-vera, il tempo de la sua fortezza e la State: il tempo
che lui comincia a esser sauio, e come l' Autunno: and la sua vechiezza, e come
l'inuerno, il qual finisce ogni cosa. Dolci sono i detti de i vechi, i loro
consigli perfetti, il loro gouerno sicuro. O come e fragile e debile giouentu,
quante citta e castella sono state ruinate, per hauer consiglio de gioueni?
quanti regni sono stati subuerti, per hauer hauuto consiglio de gioueni. Eta
come e piena di prouisione, di sapientia, di esperientia, e di studio. Il
gouerno di vna republica, non consiste solamente in forza, ma nela virtu de
lamente. Un huomo e come un pomo, che non e maturo ^l quale e brusco, ma
essendo ma^uro, diuien dolce, cosi l'huomo essendo giouine, e furioso e brusco,
non vuolp^egar, ma essendo vechio, e d^lce, humile verso tutti. Eta non ha
piacere in questo mondo, non frequenta banchetti, abhorisce libidine: quello
che piace a vechi, non piace a gioueni: quello che i vechi sanno per
esperientia, i gioueni non lo vogliono creder, ne con ingegno, ne con consigli.
O quanto mal e stato fatto per giouentu? quante republiche sono state river
sciate? quanti regni sono stati subnerti, prencipi traditi, amici ingannati.
Per la qual cosa, nela mia opinione, noi siamo meglio accettar eta, che
giouentu.

Certo io credo cosi.

I Pray you tell me, do you commend Silence, and Taciturnitie, as many have done
in the old time?

Silence is a thing worthy to be commended of all men, embraced of many, and
cherished of every estate.

Socrates did teach to his scollars three things, the first was, to be silent
in speech, the second, to be shamefaste in the face: the third, to be wise in
heart: for says he, the wisedo^e of a fool is in his tongue, and the tongue of
the wise man is hydden in his heart. Demosthenes says, that silence in a
Prince, is a necessary thing: Silence is a rare virtue, Silence is a jewel of
great valor: Thou hurtest no man, thou betrayest no man, thou deceyuest no
man, and yet Silence is the heauiest burden that is, the weightiest thought a
disease that always burns that always pricketh a man. O how few are there
found now adays among men, that hold their peace? but fewer among women.

Verily you say true, few there are found.

Tell me of courtesy, what thing Liberality is, I believe that it is a great
vice.

Wherefore say you so?

Because so few do use it.

And for that is it a vice?

What can I tell? I see so few use Liberality, that I thought it had been as
it were a kind of great treason, because it is used so seeldome.

But see you not that the world goes contrary, for where man should use
liberality, he uses avarice and covetousness. Liberality is a great virtue,
and therefore few do use it, and less do practice it. Liberality is a
virtue cherished of few, despised of many: few there are found, that in
geuyng are prompt and ready, and in taking slow: Few will believe Curtius,
when as he says, that to possess much, is no riches, but avarice and
covetousness, and contrariwise, to covet nothing, but give much, that is a
possessing of great riches, and to be liberal. Few I believe, or none at al,
there are to be found now adays, that are, as many have been in Rome; that
are frank in giving; liberal toward the most part, benefactors unto al, and
covetous toward none: such men did keep open house. Liberality in a Prince,
purchases him love and faith: in a Noble man, service and good will of all.
Liberality purchases unto al men benefits, but few say with Alexander the
Emperor.

And what says he?

He says, that a Prince should always be more prompt and ready in geuyng,
then in taking.

Oh noble saying of a prince.

An other demanded of Alexander, where were al his riches, that he had gotten
in so many wars that he had made? he made sign toward his subjects, and
answered, In the hearts of my people.

Oh noble prince.

He says, what greater thing can a Prince desire of his subjects, then truth
and faith toward him? What thing can be more laudable in a Prince, then
liberality and lenity toward his subjects? the liberality of a poor man, is
his good will. What greater gift can a man give, the^ that as proceeds from
his heart? There was a king in Thebes that was so liberal whilst he lived, that
when he dyed, there was not so much money found by him, as to bury him: he left
no money upon banks, gold in chestes, jewels in cofers, nor riches hydden.

Of those there are found few. For the more a man has, the more he ought to
fear the mutabilitie of Fortune. Every one is liberal in speech, but few are
frank in giving: every one speaks against envy and malice, and yet we hate
one another: we continually exclame against tyranny, and yet we are
mercylesse: we despise pride, and yet we are without humanity: we abhor
gluttony and drunke^nes, and yet we are always feastyng and banquettyng: we
always cry out against idleness, and yet we are always idle: we are
always speakinge evil of sclaunderous tongues, and yet we can say well of no
body. O God, at what point are we? every one does envy his neighbor, we al
seek to overcome one another.

I believe that the world be almost at an end.

So believe I also.

Certis sir you have satisfied me in many things, but yet I pray you to tell me
somethyng of Age.

Bion that Philosopher says, that Age is the last port of rest, the end of
all misery, the haven of life, the fulfyllyng of all pylgrimages, and an
honorable thing, and desired of al. There was one that asked him wherefore he
praised age so much? he answered, although the Spring be pleasant yet the
Harvest is fertile, the flowers that grow in the Spring, in Harvest are
ripened, so he compares the age of man, unto the four seasons of the year:
the time that he grows, is like the Spring: the time of his strength, is the
Summer: the time that he begins to be wise, is like the Harvest, and his age
is like winter, which finisheth al things. Sweet are the sayings of old men,
their counsel perfect, their government sure. Oh how frail and weak is
youth? how many Cities and Castles have been brought to ruin through the
counsel of young men? how many kingdoms have been subverted through young men's
counsel? Age is full of provision, of sapience, of experience, and of study.
The Rule of a common weal does not only consist in strength, but in the
virtue of the mind. A man is like an apple, that is not ripe, but sour, but
being ripe, becomes swee e: so a man buyng young, is furious and sour, and
will not bend, but being old, is sweet, and humble toward al. Age has no
pleasure in this world, frequenteth not banquettes, abhors lust: that
which pleases old men, delights not young men: that which old men know
by experience, young men will not believe it, neither with understanding nor
counsel. O how much evil has been don through youth? how many commonweals
have been overthrown? how many kingdoms subverted, Princes betrayed, friends
deceived? Wherefore in my opinion, we are better accept age, then youth.

Verily I believe so.

De li costumi de certe genti. Cap. 30. Of the manners of certain Nations.
Chap. 30.
D Itemi di gratia, se sapete i costumi di certe natione, io so che li sapete.
Tell me of courtesy, if you know the customs of certain nations, I know you
know the^. Io vi diro il piu breue che io posso: gli Ethiopij sono un certo
popolo di Caria, sono semplici, brutti, e sciaui: I Cartaginesi sono fal^i, and
ingannatori: quelli di Babilonia, sono malitiosi, and corotti: I Persiani,
ebrij, and glottoni: I Siciliani scarsi auari, and pur fedeli: quelli di Caspia
sono crudeli: quelli di Lesbia, sporchi: quelli di Scithia senzalegge: quelli
di Corintho, fornicatori: I Boetiani, molto rudi: I Simeriani, ignoranti: I
Sibariani, molto bestiali: quelli di Lacedemonia, molto arditi: gli Atheniesi,
delicati: I Romani superbi, and gloriosi: I Spagnoli, viandanti, sdegnosi, and

s^eri: I Todeschi sono guerrieri: I Sassoni, dissimulatori: quelli di Sueuia,
cianciatori: il Britanno (cio e l' Inglese,) negotratore: il Irlandese,

and cercatore di nouelle: Il Scozzese, pergiuro: Il vandallo, mutabile: Il
Bauiero, buffone. De gli aliri, non mi ricordo troppo been. I will tell you as
briefly as I can: The Ethiopians are a certain people of Caria, they are
simple, foul, and slaves: the Cartaginians are false, and deceivers: those of
Babylon, are malicious, and corrupted: the Persians are gluttonous, and
drunkardes* the Cicilians are very niggards, and yet faithful: those of Caspia
are cruel: they of Lesbia, filthy the Scithians Lawelesse: the Corinthians,

very beastly: they of Lacedemonia, very hardye: the Athenians, delicate: the

other I do not well remember. Certo viringratio per questo, di bon core. Verily
I yield you thanks for this, with a good heart.


Discorsi sopra Musica and Amor. Cap. 31. Discourses upon Music, and Love.
Chap. 31.
COme vi piace la Musica a voi? How does Music please you? La mi piace been,
and a voi. It pleases me well, and you. Musica e cosa laudabile. Music is a
laudable thing. Signor si, perch anche i Santi l'hanno vsata, come Re David,
quando che lui era malinconico, si ralegraua in sonar e cantar Salmi in laud
di Iddio suo signore. Musica si dice esser l'alegrezza di cuore: Musica
conforta il tuo animo, fa paura il nimico, perch si come Musica e piaceuole,
cosi e dolente, and anche e piena di harmonia and melodia, cosi e anche
terribile, paurosa, e spauentosa: ma ci sono molti, che la abusano hoggi di, la
qual cosa mi dispiace. Yea sir, for Saints have also used it, as king David,
when he was any thing sad, or melancholic, he took delight in playing and
singing Psalms in praise of God his lord. Music is said to be the rejoicing
of the heart: Music comfort the mind, and fears the enemy, for even as
Music is pleasant, so is it doleful, and also it is full of harmony and
melody, and also it is terrible, fearful, and terrifying: but there are many
that do abuse it now adays, which thing displeases me. Voi dite il vero. You
say true. Che cosa e amore? What is love? Amor e un dilett ^uole dispetto, and
un dispetteuole diletto. Amor honesto e ordinato da Iddio: amor dishonesto e
prohibito da Iddio, la piu difficile cosa che sia, amor la fa facile: la piu
dura cosa che sia, amor fa molle: la piu pericolosa cosa che sia, amor si
arischira per andempir il suo volere. Ma hoggi di trouerete dove vno ama
honestame^te, cento amano dishonestamente. Love is a delectable despite, and a
spiteful delight. Honest love is ordained of God: dishonest love is forbidden
of God: the difficultest thing that is, Love makes it easy: the hardest thing
that is, Love makes it soft: the most perilous thing that is, Love will
venture to bring it to pass. But now adays you shall find, that whereas one
loves honestly, an hundred love dishonestly. Veramente voi dite il vero.
Verily you say true. Non aecade parlar tanto di amore, ogni libro ^ p^eno di
amori, con tanti Autori che sarebbe pena persa, a parlar di amore. We need not
speak so much of love, al books are full of love, with so many authors, that it
were labor lost to speak of Love.


Discorso sopra libidine, and la sua forza. Cap. 32. A Discourse vton Lust, and
the force thereof. Chap. 32.
DItemi di gratia, qual e il piu gran vitio che sia piu praticato hoggi di? OF
courtesy tell me, which is the greatest vice that is most practiced now
adays? Io vi diro, mi pare che tutti i vitij sipraticano. I will tell you, me
thinks that al vices are practiced. Voi dite il vero, ma qual e il piu grande,
e di piu forza, e posanza? You say true, but which is the greatest, and of
most strength and puisance? Io credo, Libidine. I believe, Lust. Cosi credo
anche io. So believe I also. Perch Libidine e in tutti li stati, tanto ne
l'prencipe, quanto ne l'suddito, ta^to ne l'uno, come l'altro, ogniuno gli e
suggetto. Superbia principalmente ha la sua sedia fra prencipi e gran stati:
Cupidita fra gli vechij, Magistrati, and Officiali: Inuidia fra tutti i
mecanichi: Vsura fra Cittadini: Simonia fra vescoui and preti: Hipocrisia fra
gli Religiosi: Inganno fra gli Mercanti: ma libidine ha sedia in tutti questi
stati: Libidine e signor di tutto: ha vinto Signori, Dotti, Sauij, and
eloquenti: ha vinto i piu gran paladini che siano stati: Libidine fu causa che
Cleopatra giaque con suo fratello, come suo marito: fu causa, che il Re Cynare
dormi con sua figliola: Libidine fece dormir Macareo con sua sorella Canace:
f^ce che Menephron dormi con sua madre, che Oedipo giaque con sua madre, and
hebbe siglioli di essa. O crudel mostro, O bestial furor, O furia infernale,
che incanti i sauij, che inganna i dotti, che vinci i piu forti, che sottometti
ogniuno al iuo gioco. Libidine fu causa de la ruina di Troia: Libidine fu causa
che Sodoma e Gomora furono destrutte, che David pecco con Bersabe, che Amon
pecco con Thamar, che Absolon giaque con la Concubina di suo padre, che i duo
vecchi cercarono di sforzar Susanna, che Holoferno sinamoro di Judith. Libidine
fu causa che la testa fu tagliata al santo Euangelista: Libidine fu causa che
il Deluuio anego tutto il mondo, Iddio per la sua misericordia voglia aprir i
cuori de i suoi sugetti, e dar li gratia, che libidine non habbi for za
nessuna. For Lust is in all estates, as well in the prince, as in the subjects,
as much the one as the other, al me^ be subject unto it: Pride principally has
her seat amongst Princes and great Estates: Covetousness among old men,
Magistrates, and also Officers: Envy among handicraftesmen: Usury among
citizens: Simonie among Bishops and Priests: hypocrisy among the religious
and deceit among Merchants: but Lust has a seat among all Estates: Lust is
lord of al: it has overcome Lords, Learned men, Wise, and eloquent: it has
vanquished the gretest knights that have been Lust was cause, that Cleopatra
lay with her brother, as with her husband: was cause that king Cinaris lay
with his daughter: Lust made Machareus lie with his sister, Canace: it made
Menephron lie with his mother, that Oedipus lay with his mother, and had
children by her. Oh cruel monster, Oh beastly rage, Oh infernal Fury, thou
incha^test the wise, thou deceiuest the wise, thou ouerco^mest the strong, thou
subuest al men unto thy yoke. Lust was the cause that Troy was ruinated: Lust
was the cause that Sodom and Gomor were destroyed: that David sinned with
Bersabe, that Airmo^ sinned with Thamar that Absolon lay with his fathers
Concubine, that the two old men sought to ravish Susanna, that Holofernes fell
in love with Judith. Lust was the cause that the Evangelists head was cut
of. Lust was cause, that the Flood drowned all the world. God for his mercy
grant, to open the hearts of al his subrectes, and give them grace, that Lust
may have no force at al. Iddio lo acconsenti che sia cosi. God grant it be
so.


Ragionamenti sopra virtu, con le sue figlie, che cosa e il fine di Guer ca,
quale sono bone opere, quatordeci bone Regole, con altri diuersi discorsi. Cap.
33. Reasonyngs upon Virtue, with her daughters, what is the end of war,
which be good works, fourteen good Rules, with diverse other discourses.
Chap. 33.
IO ho sentuto dir molte volte, che virtu ha certe figliole, Io vo ria saper
quale sono. I Have heard say many times, that virtue has certain daughters, I
would know who they be Virtu ha quatro figliole. Virtue has four daughters.
Quale sono esse? Which be they? Forza, Prudentia, Giustitia, and Temperantia.
Force, Prudence, Justice, and Temperance. Io ho inteso che forza ha figliole
anchora lei. I have heard say, that Force has daughters also. E vero ella ne
ha quatro, come magnanimita, magnificentia, patientia, and perseuerantia, and
tutte sono bone. It is true, she has four, as Magnanimity, Magnificence,
Patience, and Perseverance, and al are good. Quale sono le figliole di
Temperantia, e quante? Which be the daughters of Temperance, and how many? Elle
sono dieci, come Vergogna, Honesta, Abstinentia, Chastita, Sobrieta,
Continentia, Clementia, Modesta, Studiosita, and Humilita. They are ten, as
Shame, Honesty, Abstinence, Chastity, Sobriety, Continency, Clemency,
modesty, Studiositie, and Humility. Quale sono le figliole di Giustitia, and
quante sono? Which be the daughters of Justice, and how many are they? Ci sono
due sort di Giustitia, come comutatiua, and distribuitiua: le sue figliole
sono quindeci, come Innocentia, Religione, Oratione, Pieta, Osseruatione,
Obedientia, Gratia, Gratitudine, Penitentia, Verita, Fedelta, Liberalita,
Misericordia, Equita, and Amicitia. There are two sorts of Justice, as
Commutatiue, and Distribuetiue: their daughters are fiftiene, as Innocence,
Religion, prayer, godliness, Observation, Obedience, Grace, Gratitude,
Penitencie, Verity, Faithfulness, Liberality, Mercy, Equity, and Amity.
Quale sono le figlie di Prudentia? Which be the daughters of Prudence? Queste
sono esse, come Dotrina, Consideratione, Bon desiderio, Intendimento, bona
Volunta, memoria, ragione, feed, Charita, and Speranza. These be they, as
doctrine, consideration, good desire, understanding, good will memory, reason,
faith, charity, and hope. Dite mi di gratia, che cosa e il fine di guerra.
Tell me of courtesy, what is the end of war. Il fine di guerra, e questo:
Iddio e bestiamato, renegato, detestato, le Chiese sono profanizate, e
sacrilegiate, le Ville brugiate, le Citta destrutte, i Castelli subuerti, le
Fortezze ruinate, gli huomini vccisi, le done for zate, le vergine violate, i
beni presi e rapiti. In guerra non ci e feed, leg, amor, humanita, humilita,
charita, ne honesta: ma oogni orgoglio, ambitione, crudelta, arogantia,
disobedientia, furto, anaritia, latrocinio, pilagio, pergiuro, fraud, impieta,
fornicatione, adulterio, ira, incesto, furor, bestemia, inuidia, vendetta,
sceleragine, ogni fur fam eria sivsa in guerra, non si harispet to a nessuo.
The end of war is this, God is blasphemed, denied, and detested, Churches
are profanizated, and sacrileged, Villages are burned, Cities destroyed,
Castles subverted, Fortes ruinated, men slain, women forced, Virgins
violated, goods taken, and rapt. In war there is no faith, law, love,
humanity, humility, charity, nor honesty: but all pride, ambition,
cruelty, arrogancy, disobedience, stealth, avarice, pilfering, pillage,
perjury, fraud ungodliness, fornication, adultery, wrath, incest, horror,
blasphemyng, envy, revenge, villainy, al knavery is used in war, there is no
respect borne to any. Queste sono buone opere: Visitar gli amalati, far
limosina a gli poueri, insegnar a li ignoranti, dar bon esempio, dar bon
consiglio, consolar gli aslitti, insegnar bona dottrina, non scandalizar
nessuno, cercar il profitto del tuo prossimo, perch lo comanda iddio honorar
'Dio, ben edirlo, laudarlo, magnisicarlo, and esaltarlo. These are good works
to visit the sick, to do al^nese unto the poor, to teach the ignorant, gene
good example, give good counsel comfort the afflicted, teach good doctrine,
sclaunder no body, seek the profit of thy neighbor, for God commands it,
honor God, bless him, pray se him, magnify him and exalt him. Quatordeci
bone Regole per adornar un Gentilhuomo: esser sempre humile di cuore: parlar
poco, eragioneuolme^te: non esser pronto e facile a ridere: tacer sia che un
sia interogato: tener i Statuti, and or li^^ de i tuoi superiori, tanto
spiritueali, come Te^porali: farc nto di esser il minor de tutti: tien ti i de
no di far ogni cosa: Confessa i tuoi peccati a Dio: Habbi paci^ntia in
aduersita: Sottometti ti a tuoi superiori: Vinci il t uo volere: accorda al'
opinione del sau^o: va ves^^to mother at amente: prega Iddio, and credi n^l
padre, nel sigliolo, and nel spirito sarto, e non cercar di esser saluato per
altro mezo che per lui. Fourtiene good rules for to adorn a Gentleman: to be
always humble in heart: to speak little, and reasonably: be not ready and
prone to laugh: hold thy peace, till thou be asked: keep the statutes of thy
superiors, as well Spiritual, as Temporal: account thy self the least of
al: account thy self unworthy to do any thing acknowledge thy sins to God:
have patience in adversity: submyt thy self to thy superiors: vanqui he thine
own will: agree with the opinion of the wise: apparel thy self moderately:
pray God, and belene in the father, the son, and in the holy ghost and seek
not to be saved by any other means, then by him. Io vi prego, ditemi qua li
sono li nimici de le quatro virtu morale. I pray you tell me, which be the
enemies of the four moral virtues. Ce ne sono assai, come pergiuro,
disordine, follia, afett^one, apetito, fastidio mondano, concupiscentia,
orgoglio, ambitione, simulatione, hipocrisia, tra limento, ingratitudine,
mentire, insacieta, superbia, obliuione, gelosia, vtolentia, desperatione,
importunita, dispetto, furore, arogantia, malitia, and inuidia. There are a
great many, as perjury disorder folly, fond affection, appetite, worldly care,
concupiscence, wrath, ambition, dissimulation, slattery, hypocrisy, treason,
ingratitude, lying, insacietie, pride, forgetfulness, jealousy, violence,
desperation, importunity, despite, horror, arrogancy, malice, and envy. I
Ministri di aduersit a sono, Tentatione, infirmita, ignorantia, guerra, peste,
fame, processo, discordia, perdita d'amici, fastidio, and diuer si accidenti,
and altri inconuenienti. The Ministers of adversity are, Temptation,
sickness, ignorance, war, plague, hunger, process, discord, loss of
friends, care, and diverse accidents, and other in conueniences. I ministri di
prosperita sono, Innocentia, humilita, specialta, favor, pieta, conscientia,
amor, discretione, benignita, integrita, amicitia, sede hon desiderio, gratia,
and timor di Dio. The ministers of prosperity are, Innocence, humility,
specialtie, favor, pitic, conscience, lone, discretion, benignity,
integrity, amity, says, good desire, grace, and fear of God. Questi sono i
messi di peccato, Sugestiune, cogitatione, diletto, banchetto, festino,
dissolutione giuoco, and ballo, follia, piacere, prodigalita, inconstantia,
inconsideratione, et cattiua compagnia. These are the messengers of syn,
Suggestion, cogitation, delight, banqueting, feasting, dissolution, play, and
dau^sing, folly, pleasure, prodigality, inconstancy, inconsideration, and
evil company. Questi sono i cinque sensi di Natura, cio e, Vedere Se^tire,
Toccare, Gustare, et odorare, ma guarda come, quando, quale, che, and dove.
These are the five senses of Nature, that is to say, To See, to Hear, to
Feel, to Taste, to Smell, but look how, when, which, what, and where. Quatro
sort di tentatione, Concupiscentia, ambitione, Hipocrisia, and vana speranza.
Four sorts of temptation, as Concupiscence, ambition, hypocrisy, and vain
hope. Quatro humori regnano nel'huomo, secondo i quatro clementi, cio e, Vna
complessione e sanguigna, l'altra cholericha, l'altra flemmaticha, l'ultima
Malinchonicha. Four humors reign in man, according to the four Elements,
that is to say, One complexion is sanguine, an other cholerik, an other
flegmatike, and the last, melancholic. Si vede de gli huomini che diuent ano
poueri per tree cause: alcuni per esser troppo pietosi: ma de quelli ce ne
pochi. alcuni per esser troppo liberali: de quelli ce ne ma^co, alcuni per
esser troppo prodighi: ma de quelli ce ne assai in Londra. There are men, that
becomme poor through three causes: some because they are too pitiful: but of
those there are few. Some for being too liberal: of those there are less:
some other for being to prodigal: of those there are a great many in London.
Da tree cose guardate, cio e, Da un Sicopha^te, da un Adulatore, da un
presuntuoso. Of three things beware, that is, of a Sicofant, of a flatterer,
and of a presumptuous man. Tree cose bone in un prencipe, Misericordia,
Eloquenza, and Dotrina. Three good things in a prince, mercy, eloquence, and
godliness. Vna cosa in tree fal'huomo saluo da ogni male, Padre, Figliolo et
lo, spirito santo. One thing in three makes man safe from al evil, Father,
Son, and holy ghost. I tree corsi del'huomo, Pueritia, Giouentu, and Vechieza.
The three courses of man, chyldishnes, youth, and age. Tree bone Pegole per
ogniuno, Reggi il tuo volere: tempera la tua lingua: rafrena il tuo ventre.
Three good rules for every man Rule thine own will, temper thy tongue, refrain
thine own belly. Tree bone cose: Verita, charita, e virtu. Three good things,
truth, charity, and virtue. Tree sort di flagello, Fame, peste, and guerra.
Three sorts of destruction, Famine, plague, and war. Tree cose bone per vna
donna, le richezze di Giuno, la sapi^tia di Pallas, la belezza di Cerere. Three
good things in a woman, The riches of Juno, the wisdom of Pallas, the beauty
of Venus. Tree sort di eloquentia, Gramatica Retorica, Dialettica. Three sorts

L'opinione di Mar co Aurelio, and Ouidio, sopra amore, et che cosa e. Chap.
34. The opinion of Marcus Aurelius, and Ovid, upon Love, and what it is. Chap.
34.
DItemi di gratia (se vi pia ce) la vostra opinione in questa cosa, se vi par
meglio chel, huomo ami donna, o no. I Pray you tell me (if you please) your
opinion in this thing, If you think best, that a man love a woman or no. Io vi
diro, lui puo amare, se li piace, ma grandi and innumerabili sono i pericoli, a
che si sottomotte l'huomo che conuersa con done, and grande e il riposo and
quiet, che viene a quelli che del tutto le abandonano. I will tell you, he may
love, if he please, but great and innumerable are the dangers, that he submyts
him self unto, that does hant women, and great is the rest and quietness that
comes unto those that altogether abandon them. Come puo essere, che ne riesca
affanno and dolor in amar donna, and ripeso and quiet in non amarle. How can
that be that there may come care and grief in loving a woman, and rest and
quietness in not loving them. Sapete come dice Marco Aurelio, gia Imperator di
Roma? Know you how Marcus Aurelius, sometimes Emperor of Rome, says? Signor
no, per cortesia ditemelo. No sir* of courtesy tell me. Lui dice, parlando di
questo ponto, se l'huomo non ama donna, sui e reputato villa^o: se luile a na,
lo tengono legero^ se lui le lascia, persano che lui si ^ timido, e cotardo: se
lui le s^gue, lui e person: se lui le serve, non fanno contodi lui: se lui non
le serve, lohanno in grand' odio: se lui le vuole hauere, non lo vegliono hauer
lui: se lui non le desidera, lo cercano: se lui gli t^en compagnia, lui perde
il suo nome: se lui non gli t^en compagnia, pens^no che non sia huomo: se lui
le cerca, lo fuggono: se lui non le cerca, lo vanno ^ercando: lui: se lui le
ad'ora, lo disprezano: se lui non le adora, piangono) che cosa volete dunche
che ^acci il misero huomo, se non del tutto abandonarle? He says, speaking of
this point, if a man love not a woman, he is reputed as a villayne: if he love
the^, he is counted light: if he leave them, they think him to be fearful and
a dastord: if he follow them, he is lost: if he serve the^ they make no account
of him: if he serve the^ not they hate him greatly: if he will have them, they
will not have him: if he do not desire them, they seek him: i^ he keep them
company, he loses his name: if he keep the^ not company, they think him no
man: if he seek them, they slay him: if he seek them not, they go seeking
him: if he woorshyp them, they despise him: if he do not woorship them, they
weep: what will you that the wretched man do, but altogether forsake them: Ma
Iddio ha comandato, che l'huomo debbe lasciar padre, e madre, fratelli e
sorelle, per acostarsi a donna. But God commanudeth, that man shall leave father
and mother, sister, and brother, to join him self with a woman. E vero ogni
volta, che lei sia pudica and honesta, perch il tener compagnia a donna, non
essendo sua legitima moglie, e tenuto gran peccato, come e vero. It is true, so
she be honest and chaste: for to keep company with a woman, if s^ee be not his
lawful wife, it is counted a great sin, as it is in deed. Ma ditemi di
gratia, d'onde viene questo amore, che gli huomini portano all done? Ho
setuto d^re, che fino li dei hanno amato: amor ha fatto diuentar homini sauij,
pazi: homini dotti, ignoranti: homini forti, deboli. D'onde viene questa cosa.
But tell me of courtesy, whence comes this love, that men ^eare unto women? I
have heard say, that the gods have loved: Love has made wise men become
fools, learned men ignorant, strong men weak: from whence comes this thing?
Come volete che io lo sappi, quando che gli dotti, sauij, and forti, and anche
li Dei non lo hanno potuto sapere. Ouidio dice, cosi, amore e, no^ so che cosa,
lui viene non so d'onde, chi l'ha mandato, io no^ lo so: lui genera, io non so
come: lui e contento, io non so de che: amor si sent, non so quanto spesso.
Amor mi da fastidio, non so perch: amor mi penetra la carne, and si non sparge
sangue: lui piglia radice nel mio core, and non mi rompe carne. Che ingegno,
che sapientia, che forza, che dotrina, che inganno, che fortuna mi puo difender
da amore? io non so certo. How will you have me know it, when learned men, wise,
and strong, yea gods could not know it? Ovid says, that Love is, I wot not
what, he comes, I wot not whence, who has sent him, I know not: he
ingendreth, I know not how: he is co^te^ted I know not with what: Love is felt,
I know not how often: love trobleth me, I know not wherefore: love does pierce
my flesh, and shedeth no blood: he takes root in my heart, and breaks no
flesh. What engine, what wisdom, what strength, what learning, what deceit,
what fortune can defend me from Love? I know not certain.


Diuersita de gli huomini. Cap. 35. The Diversities of men. Chap. 35.
SE lui e rustico, nessuno tien conto di lui ^ se lui e di alto sangue, lui e
santuoso: se lui e ricco, lui e vitioso: se lui e pouero, lui e auaro: se lui e
valente, lui e trogpo ardito: se lui e cotardo, lui e defamato: se lui e
parlatore, lui mente: se lui parla poco, e tenuto pazzo: se lui e bello, lui e
desiderato: se lui e brutto, lui e geloso: se si intramette, e chiamato
importuno: se lui tace, e tenuto semplice: se lui ride, e detto ignorante: se e
liberale, e tenuto prodigo: se non e liberale, e tenuto scarso and auaro.
Vedendo si questo, vdendo si questo, and sapendosi questo, che fara l'huomo
tristo, doloroso, and spectalmente se ha prudentia: perch se si vuol separare
dal mondo, la carne non li da licentia, and se vuol seguire il mondo, il suo
cuore non lo consent. Pensino tutti gli huomini che hanno a contentar ciascuno
con seruitu, and beneuolenza: perch to gli fo a sapere, che may non
contenteranno tutti gli huomini, Anchora che faccia qua^to puo come huomo, and
faccia quanto deue, come bon vicino, and che caui forza de la deboleza con
molta fatica and rimedij alla pouerta, coni suoi sudori, and si ponga per
compiacer a ciascuno in ogni pericolo, al sine non potra may contentar tutti:
da alcuni sara amato, da alcuni odiato, da alcuni ripreso, da alcuni adulato:
se lui fa been, e inuidiato da molti: se lui fa male, e disprezato da assai. O
pouero homo, se tu hauessi tutti gli ochi di Argo, le mani di Briaro, la
sapientia di Salomone, con le ricceze di Mida, and la eloquentia di Cicerone,
and le belezze di Assolone: tu non compiacerai may tutti.

Certo voi dite il vero.

IF he be rustical, none account of him: if he be high of blod, he is
sumptuous: if he be rich, he is vicious: if he be poor, he is covetous: if he
be valiant, he is too hardye: if he be a dastard, he is defamed: if he be a
tatler, he is a liar: if he speak little, he is counted a fool: if he be
fair, he is desired: if he be foul, he is ielous: if he be a medler, he is
called importune: if he hold his peace, he is counted simple if he laugh, he is
called ignorant: if he be liberal, he is deemed prodigal* if he be not liberal,
he is counted covetous and scarce. Man seeing this, hearing this, and knowing
this, what shall the poor and wretched man do, and specially if he have any
wisdom? for if he will separate him self from the world, the flesh will not let
him: and if he will follow the world, his heart co^senteth not to it. Let al men
think, that have to content every one with service and benevolence: for I let
them to know, that they shall never content all men, although he do as much as
man can do, and though he do as much as he ought, like a good neighbor, and
that he take force from weakness, with much labor and remedy unto poverty,
with his sweat, and put him self in all peryls only to please al me^, at the
last he shall never content all men. of some he shall be loved, of some hated, of
some checked, of some flattered: if he do well, he is enuyed of many: if he do
il, he is despised of many more. Oh poor man, if thou had all the eyes of
Argos, the hands of Briarius, the wisdom of Solomon, with the riches of
Midas, and the eloquence of Cicero, and the beauty of Absalon, thou shall
never please al men.

Verily you say true.

Certi belli, dotti, et galanti detti, tolti da Antonio Gueuara, scritti da
lui, sopra diverse ocasioni. Cap. 36. Certain fine, learned, and gallant
sayings, taken from Antonio Gueuara, written by him, upon diverse occasions.
Cha. 36.
ANtonio Gueuara chiama Dio, quel solo verbo diuino, vnigenito del padre,
signor perpetuo delle Gierarchie, piu antico di tutti i secoli, prencipe delle
eternita, principio dal quale ogni cosa hebbe principio, primo genito di Dio,
creatore di tutte le creature, saluatore di tutti i dispersi, Sacerdote
vniuerso, Intercessore per tutti i peccatori, and redentore di tutta l'humaua
gente: habbi mi sericordia di tutti noi. ANtonio Gueuara calls God, the only
divine word, only begotten of the father, everlasting Lord of the Gierarchies,
more ancient the^ al ages, prince of al eternities, beginning, from who^ al
things had beginning, first begotten of God, creator of al other creatures,
Savior of all the dispersed, universal priest, Intercessor for all sinners,
and redeemer of all the humane people, have mercy upon us all. Il medesimo in
laud de la chiedi Dio. The self same in praise of the Church of God. O
gloriosa Chiesa militante la qual non sei altro che Oro ne la feccia, Rosa tra
la spine, grano tra la paglia, midolla tra le ossa, perla tra le conche marine,
anima santain carne putrida, vnica fenice ne la cabbia, naue tra le fiere onde
ingolfata, la quale tanto piu e combatuta tanto piu si mostra gagliarda, tu non
mostri may la tua potentia, seron quando sei da tuoi nimici ofesa, and sei
ofesa da i piu potenti Re, and defesa da i piu deboli and poueri, Iddio per la
sua gratia longo tempo ti mantenga and difenda. Oh glorious militant Church,
which art naught else but gold in dross, a rose amongst thorns, grain amidst
straw, marow amidst bones, pearl amidst the sea shelles, a holy soul in
rotten flesh, one only Fenix in the cage, a ship amidst the fierce waves
engolfed, which the more it is striue^ withal, the stronger thou show
thyself thou show not thy might, but only when thou art of thine enemies
offended, and art offended by the mightiest kings, and defended of the weakest
and poorest. God for his grace long time maintain and defend thee. Il detto in
laud de Iddio. The same in praise of God. Io confesso and dico con la
religione cristiana, che non vi e altro che un solo Dio, creatore del Cielo,
and de la terra, della cui eccelentia, potentia, maesta, and gloria, poco ne
puo esp^m^re la lingua nostra, perch non puo il nostro ing gno comprendere le
cose divine, ne il nostro intellet to discernerle, ne la nostra memoria
ricordarsene, and molio meno la nostra lingua dimostrarle, ne la nostra me^te
capirle, and da lui, per ui, and in lui, credo ottene^ saluatione. I confess,
and say with the Christian Religion, that there is but one only God, creator
of heaven and earth, of whose excellency, puisance, majesty, and glory, our
tongue can little express, for our wit and reason can not comprehend the
Divine things, neither our vnderstandying discern them, neither our memory
remember it, and much less our tongue show it, neither our mind conceive it:
and by him, through him, and in him, I hope to obtain salvation. Poco apresso
dice in laud de Dio, Io dico and confesso, che non ci e che vno solo Dio
creatore, perch creo tutto il mondo: potente, perch ha potentia in Cielo, and
in terra: Immortale, perch non hebbe may principio, and may non hauera fine:
incorutibile, perch non patisce ne corutione, ne diminuitione, immutabile,
perch non muta may l'eterno conseglio: inconprensibile, perch la sua maesta
non si puo conoscere, no la sua potentia intendere: onnipotente, perch ha
potentia sopra vini and morti, sopra buoni and cattiui: and l'huomo non ha da
adorar che vno solo Dio, perch si come il mondo fu creato per vno solo, cosi
tutte creature deuono adorare vno solo, perch si come un prencipe non consent
che un altro si chiami prencipe nel suo regno, cosinon vuol Iddio che si aderi
