A great many other like parcels I leave behind, which are in this Italian
tongue, mary not so much used. Wherefore I leave them, as more tedious, then
pleasant, or profitable, either for the learner of it, or any other. And thus
will I end concerning Nounes, and God willing we will come to the Pronounes.

Of the Pronounes.
BY the Pronounes did diverse ancient Authors understand certain determined
voices, the which do as it were show forth, or demonstrate the name of a
thing, and divided them into sundry and diverse parts, calling some Primatiues,
some Deriuatiues, other some Demonstratiues, some Relatiues, some Possessiues,
some Interrogatiues, and Indefinitiues.

There are also other divisions found among them, which be good for us: our
Primitiues are, Io, I, tu, thou, se, egli, e, ej, and lui, for he, colui,
qvello, for that he, or that same, and qvegli, qvelli, qvei, and qve', for
those same, or them same, qvesto esto, this he, qvesta esta, this ^hee, costui,
cotesto, cotestui, this same he, che, what, chi, and cui, for who, or whom,
qvale, and il qvale, for which: esso, desso, and stesso, for him self,
cjascuno, and cjascaduno, for each one, and every body, veruno, and niuno, for
no one, and nessuno, for no body, alcuno, and qvalcuno, for some one, or some
body, altri, altro, and altrui, for other or others, cjo, and cjoche, this, and
sometimes yea, many times, cjoe, and cjoche, stands for, so that, or what so
ever, tale, such, or the like, medesimo, self, or else, self same.

The Derived are these, as Mio, mine, tuo, thine, suo, his, nostro, ours,
vostro, yours, loro, they, cui, and altrui, are sometimes used for the same: of
which there are certain bastardes derived, as Mo for now, ma but, to, thine,
ta, thine, speaking to a woman, so, his, of the which, somewhat we will speak
hereafter.

But it is to be noted, that unto the Pronounes, there is no Article given,
except it be to, il medesimo, the self same, and il qvale, the which, (who
are Relatiues,) but only the signs of Cases. And also some there are, that
are without it, as you shall know hereafter.

You are also to know, that some pronounes are of each gendre, and these are
they, Io, tu, se, loro, coloro, costoro, cotestoro, cui, and altrui: all the
others, are some of the Masculine only, and some of the Feminine only.

It is also to be remembered, that the first person is of the pronoune alone,
Io, and the second of the alone, tu, and all the rest belongs to the third,
now let us come to the particulars.

NV. SIN. ENGLI. NV, PLV. ENGLI. Io, and i, are never but of the first case,
the other of the obliques, and noi, is of all cases. 1. Io. i. I. 1. noi. we.
2. di me of me. 2. di noi. of us. 3. ame, mi, m', to me. 3. a noi, ne, ce. ci,
c'. to us. 4. me, mi, m'. me. 4. noi. us or we. 6. da me. from me. 6. da noi.
from us.

NV. SIN. ENGLI. NV. PLV. ENG. Tu, may be of the first, and fifth case and of
no other but the other are of others, and voi, is of all cases, tu. 1 thou. voi
1 you di te. 2. of thee. di voi 2 of you ate, ti, to 3. to thee, a voi, ve, vi,
v', 3 to you te, ti, to 4. thou voi, ve, vi, v', 4 you o tu. 5. o thou o voi, 5
o you da ie. 6. from thee da voi, 6 from you NV. SIN. English. NV. PLV.
English. This se, never does vary neither case nor number, in the third case
it stands sometimes without the sign, as: Chi porta l'ume djetro e se non
gjova, who caryeth the light behind and helps not him self. We see certain
words in these three pronounes end in, t and in, e, which are, mi, ti, si. ci,
vi, and so, me, te, se, ce, ve, when they end in i. they are joined with a
Verb, either before, or after it, if before, then are they sometimes
apostrophed, sometimes not, if after it, then are they entermingled with it,
under an accent, and if the accent be open, the latter letter then is dobled.
di se 2 of him, di se, 2 of them a se, se, si, s' 3 to him, a se, 3 to them se,
si, s' 4 him, se si, 4 them da se, 6 from him da se, 6 from the^,

Now because these voices are only of the third and fourth case, let us see
first those of the third: as, mi pjace, or else pjacemi, and m'e caro, it
pleases me, ti gjova, gioavti, and t'aggrada, it avails thee or helps
thee, si compjace, compjacesi, and s'appaga, he is pleased or well apaide, ci
diletta, dilettaci, and c'e grato, it pleases, or delights us, vi gjova
giovavi, and v'abbella, it helps, or avails you, si compiaecjono,
compiaccjonsi, and s'apagano, they are either pleased or else well apaied.

Those of the fourth case are such, mi preme, prememi, and m'aggrava, it
greeueth me, ti cvoce, cvoceti, and t'abbrugja, it boyleth, or burns thee, si
mostra, mostrasi, and s'asconde, he shows and hides himself, ci preme,
premeci, and c'agrava, it greeueth us, vi cvoce, cvocevi, and v'abbrugia, it
boyleth and burns you, si mostrano, mostransi, and s'ascondono, they show
and hide themselves. And if the accent be upon the last, we say dammi give
me, and many such: As for the others as we have said before in the dobblinges.

But you are to note, and mark, that you fall not into that error, to join
these little parcels, with the Verbes, as some do very disorderly, for when
they should say, se ti manchera cosa alcuna, if thou want any thing, or else,
ti daro la promessa, se ti trovero, I will give thee, the promise, if I find
thee: they will say, se manchera^ti cosa alcuna, darotti la promessa se
troueuotti: which is very ill, he tl at knows no other rule, rather then to
fall in this foul error, let him never join them, and if he will do it
without blame, let him do it unto other Verbes, and not in those, as, lo lo
trovai, e dissigli il fatto mio, I found him, and told him my business or else,
Io mi parti di la, e ridussimi a casa, I parted from thence and brought me
self home.

It is also to be known, that many times, such parcels, are more used for an
ornament, and fulfilling of the speech, then for necessity, as thus, Egli si
creed, e tu ti pensi, che ogniuno che ci nasce, he believes, and thou do
think, that each one that is borne, and many such more, if they be well set
together they make the speech to show, and seem more pleasant, and gallant,
as, Io mi ti racomando, I commend me to thee, tu miti mostrasti aronto, thou
showedst thy self to me prompt, eglij mi si proferj da se, he did proffer him
self to me io mj ci fermai, I did stay my self there, tu ti ci facesti
incontro, thou of thy self came against, or toward me, ej ci si offerce, he
offered him us, and many other such which do commonly end in, i. But if they
end in e, either it is when they are not joined with the verb, as, se non me
le darai, io te le pigliero, if thou geuest them not me, I shall take them from
thee, or else if they be joined they have the little parcel with it, as,
Dammene, give me thereof, Andjancene, go we, pentitevene, repent you of it,
accorsersene, they perceived themselves, or else there is as it were some
comparision; as Io lodo te, e tu bjasimime; I praise thee, and thou blamest
me: or else there follows some infinitive, as, Io av^vo inteso, te esser,
risanato, e questa nuova fece me tardare il viaggio: I heard say that thou wast
healed again, and this news made me to stay the voyage, moreover they may
end in, i, and in, e, when they follow either, l, or else, r, as, calmi,
calm, valmi, valme, parmi, parme, darmi, darme. It is also good to be noted,
not to use in the common course of speech to set any of these little parcels
before the Infinitiues, nor Gerondes, nor Participles, nor Supines, and say, mi
fare, tidire, si mostrando, vinarrante, m'insegnante, si pentito, for it is
abominable.

In this language, they are always put behind, saying, farmi, dirti,
monstradosi, narrantevi, pentitosi, sometimes also these little parcels end in e
and i, and happens when they are joined with Subiunctiue articles, or else
Pronounes, as, raccomandos segli and raccomandoglisi, he recommended himself
to him; portaitegli, or else portaigliti; I brought them to thee, and such
others, which for want of time I pass over. Now let us come to the rest.

NV. SIN. ENGLISH. NV. PLV. ENGLISH. Egli, e, ei, 1 He Eglino. 1 they, or them.
di lui 2 of him, or his. di loro, loro. 2 of them, or theirs a lui, lui, gli
li. 3 to him. a loro, loro, gli, li. 3 to them. lui, il, lo, 'l, l'. 4 he, or
him loro, gli, li. 4 they, or them. da lui. 6 from him da loro. 6 from the^.
NV. SIN. ENGL. NV. PLV. ENGL. Ella, la. 1 She, or her. Elleno. 1 They, or
them. di lei. 2 of her, or hers. di loro, loro. 2 theirs, or of them. a lei,
lei, le. 3 to she, or her. a loro, loro, gli. 3 to them. lei, la. 4 she, or
her. loro, le. 4 they, or them. da lei. 6 from her. da loro. 6 from them.

Egli, eglino, ella, elleno, e, and ei, are of the first Case, but lui, lei,
loro, are of the Obliques, and yet they are otherwise used of the common sort.

In the third Case you may say, Io dissi a lui, io dissi lui, io gli dissi, and
io li dissi, for I told him.

And in the fourth, Io vidi lui, io il vidi, and io 'l vidi, for, I saw him,
Io lo intesi, and Io l' intesi, I understood him.

And as well in the singular, as in the plural may you say, E mi vide, and ei mi
vide, he saw me: e mi videro, and ei mi videro, they saw me.

Of the Feminine Gender, in the plural number you may say, Ella mi disse, she
told me: s' ella m' avesse detto, if she had told me: Se la fosse viva, if
she were alive.

And in the third Case, Io dissi a lei, io dissi lei, and Io le dissi, I told
her.

And in the fourth, Io vidi lei, io la vidi: I saw her.

In the greater number they are al Obliques, as well the Masculine, as the
Feminine, and in this wise they often loose the last vowel, and often times
they are found without the sign of the second and third Case, saying: le lor
mogli, their wives, i lor mariti, their husbands, Io diedi a loro, io diedi
loro, io gli diedi: I gave them.

But in the fourth case we say, Vidi loro, gli vidi, li vidi: I saw them. And
yet in the Feminine Gender we only say: le vidi. Egli and e are often used,
for to make up the word, in the end: lui and lei are used as fourth Cases,
after the Verb Essere, to be, as S' io fossi lui, if I were he, S' io fosse
lei, if I were she. And also after this word Come, as, or such like, saying,
Io son ricco come lui: I am as rich as he: Io son grosso come lei: I am as
gross as she: We also say, S' io fossi come te, If I were as thou.

And after the absolute Geroundes, as Andando lui, he going. But if a Verb did
follow, we should say, Andando egli, s' incontro in un suo amico: he going, met
with a friend of his. Also, lui, lei, and loro, are often used of good
Authors, in stead of Colui, colei, coloro, and that happens, when Che does
follow, as, Invoco lei, che ben sempre rispose. We have a great many more, as,
qvello, qvell', qvel, qvegli, qvelli, qvei, for that, and qvelli, qvegli, qvei,
and qve' for those: The which to decline, I think it but labor lost.
Wherefore, if you would know how to decline them I pray you take Scipio
Lentuloes Grammar, where he writes very well and learnedly of the Pronounes,
far better then I can do: wherefore I pass them over, but yet not
altogether.

Qvegli and qvelli are not used in the singular case, but only in the
relation: and it were better, if they were not used at al in the singular,
because they make the plural doubtful.

Note also, that those words that end in lli, often times change the first l
into g, as qvelli those, belli fair, fratelli brethren: you may say, qvegli,
or begli, or frategli. And when qvello is without company, it signifies Qvella
cosa, that thing: as qvel che si dice, that which is said, or else, qvel che
si fa, that which is done, which does as it were accord with the Latin
Neuter.

Many other little odd words, that in other places are Articles, here they
are Pronounes, as Io il presi e lo stracejai, or else, presilo, e straccjailo, I
took him, and tore him: and so, gli presi et straccjai, or else, presili, et
straccjaili, I took them and tore them, and so, jo la vidi, and vidila, I saw
her, and Io le vidi, and vidile, I saw them, and as for, qvesto, and esto,
this, and qvesti, these, of the Masculine, and qvesta, esta, and sta, this, and
qveste, these, of the Feminine, and of cetesto, cotestui, and costui, for this
same, cotesti, cotestoro, and costoro, for these, and cotesta, and costs, for
this, she, and coteste, for these she is, I pray you look in Scipio Lentulo, as
for Che, it never changes voice, but always is a like in every Gender, in all numbers, and cases. as, Il Dio che io adoro, The God that I worship, La
bonta che e in lui, the goodness that is in him, I cjeli che risplendeno, the
stars that shine, La terra che scalda il sole, the earth that the sun warmes,
Qvello che no^ fece may persona, that which never person did. And here it is as
it were a member, and has that nature, sometimes we find it to bear an
article, or else a sign of the case, and then does it signify, cosa, a thing,
as, Ej dice, di voler volare, il che da maraviglia ad ognjuno. He says he
will fly, which thing causes each one to wonder, Di che cjascuno si fa beffe:
Which thing every one iesteth at. Al che niuno acconsente. To which thing none
co^senteth. Il che niuno non creed: The which thing none believes, Da che
ognjuno si gvarda: Of which thing every one takes heed. Or else, Elle dicono
cose, che fanno maravigljare ogniuno: They tell things, that makes each one
wonder. Di che cjaseun si maraviglia: Of which thing each man does marvel. A
che niuno da feed. To which none gives credit. Che niuno creed. That no body
believes. Da che si part ognjuno: From which each one departs.

In the third case, being without sign, or article; it is interogatiue, as,
Che te ne pare? what think thou of it?

Many other things mi^^t be said of this word che, and of the diverse
significations of it, which I pass over for want of time, and also I have
told you above, the sundry and diverse meanings of it.

I pray you tell me something about this word, che, and especially how it is
pronounced.

Che, is pronounced in Italian, even as we pronounce our ke in English, and
chi, even as kee, if e and i alone follow c, then pronounce it shi, or she, but
if a and o follow it, then pronounce it ka, or ko, and so forth.

Surely this is a good rule, but now follow on your pronounes.

Here are also to be remembered chi, and cui, who, are also Relatiues, and
interogatiues, as chi hai tu visto: who have thou seen. Also of chiunqve,
whosoever, but that has the nature of the latin neuter also, il qvale, and li
qvale, of the Feminine, which with the articles are relatiues, and without
interogatiue, and Demonstratiue, and often they loose the last vowell as, Tuil
qvale ti di lletti de la musica; thou the which delightest in music, voi i
qvali vi di lettate del canto; you the which delight in the song, di qval di
voi e l'onor; of the which of you is the honor. Here is also, esso, which
sometimes means he, or himself, and essi, they or themselves, essa, hirselfe
or she, and esse, they or themselves, and they serve for all cases, and
numbers, but it often ioynes with other pronounes, and nounes substantives, as,
con esso me: with me self, con esso voi: with your self, con esso loro: with
themselves, then is there, ste sso, stesse, ste ssa, and stesse, and desso,
dessi, dessa, desse, which do accord much with esso, essi, essa, esse, then
have we among the pronounes, qvalche, which signifies sometimes, some, and
with the homo, and volta donna: or some such thing it signifies, some man,
some thing, some woman, as qvalche homo, qvalche donna, qvalche cosa: it
serves all Genders, it has no plural number, and it must have a substantive,
the^ have we cjascuno, cjascuna, cjascheduno, and cjascheduna, which is, each
one, or else, each, all the which are without plural each of them may be clipped
of the last vowell in writing, as, cjascun fedel: each faithful, or cls,
cjascadun tiranno, each: tirant, some will use catanno, or ess, cadauno, but it
is filthy, and not to be used, then have we ogniuno, ogniuna, that is, each
one, or else every one which does altogether agree with cjascuno, or cjascuna,
then have we veruno, and veruna, for no one, it has no Plural, in the
Masculine it may be clipped of the last vowell, then have we also niuna, and
niuna, of the self same meaning that veryno is but only that niuno goes
always in the beginning of any saying, as, N'iun u' era present, There was no
one present, niuna persona, no one person, it may be either clipped, or
apostrophed, which you please, and thereafter as occasion serves, then have we
nessuno, and nessuna, for no body, or not one, you may write it nessuno, and
nissuno, and nessun, for it has no plural, then have we nullo, and nulla,
which sometimes is set among the nounes, sometimes among the pronounes, Nullo,
and Nulla, may upon occasion be set with the substantive, but most times, it
is of the neuter, and sometimes it signifies nothing, and sometime, something,
as, Tunon fainulla, thou do nothing, or else, volete voi nulla: will you
something, or any thing, Poss'io nulla pervoi, can I any thing or something for
you, then have we qvalcuuo, and qvalcuna, alcuno, and alcuna for some, or, some
one, or some body, it may also loose the last vowell, it may either be alone,
or with company, as, Alcuni animali, sono terreni, alcuni aqvatici: alcuni
aerei, some creatures are earthy, some watrie, and some of the air. Then have
we qvalunqve, or chiunqve, for who so ever, or what so ever, they serve for
both Genders, but that qvalunqve, can not be without a Substantive, and may be
applied either to a person, or a thing, as, qvalunqve pjanta: what plant so
ever, qvalunqve huomo, what man so ever, and chiunqve can not be put to
things, but to a person, as, chiunqve chjede ricev^ who so ever asks
receives, chiunqve cercatrova, whosoever seeks finds.

Then have we altro other, altri others, the Masculine, and altra and altre the
Feminine. If l be put before it, as l' altro, then it signifies the other: and
gli altri the others. Then have we alirui, signifying sometimes others, and
sometimes other men: as per colpa altrui, for other men's fault, or else, Io
lascio tal carico ad alirui: I leave such a charge to others.

Then have we cjo, signifying this, as cjo mi piace: this pleases me. etc.
Then have we cjoche, sometimes it means any thing, sometimes every thing,
sometimes what so ever. Many ways it may be applied, for they are two
Pronounes joined together. This cjo is accompanied many times, with sundry
voices, and has sundry meanyngs, as accjo to the end, accjoche to the end,
percjo therefore, and sometimes because, olirea cjo moreover, or besides this,
sopra cjo over, or upon this, senza cjo without this, contutto cjo for all this,
percjoche for because, and many such other like.

Then have we for the singular tale, and tali, tai, and ta' for the plural, for
such, or the like. This Pronoune somtymes is with an Article, somtymes without
it, when with it, it is Demonstratiue, and without it, Relative. Sometimes it
is used whole, and sometimes clipped, as I have partly told you before. When
it is with an Article, then do we say, Il tale m' anoia: such a one annoyeth
me: la tale mi p^ace: such a one pleases me. Here it is Demonstratiue: but
Rolatiue it is thus, Qvale me la djedi, tale la recevei: Such as he gave, such
I received.

When it is Indefinitiue, sometimes it answeareth che, as Tal vi su che non vi
rorebbe esser stato: Such a one was there, as would not have been there. And
sometimes it is joined with che, as Talche due bestie van sotto una pelle: In
such wise, or, so that two beasts go under one skin.

Then have we also among the Pronounes, Il medesimo the self same, of the
Masculine Gender, and the singular number, and i medesimi, the self same, of
the Plural number: la medesima, the self same, of the Feminine Gender, and
singular humber: le medesime, of the Feminine Gender, and Plural number.
Sometimes it is joined with other Pronounes, and Substantive Nounes, and
sometime without an Article, as Io medesimo, I me self, Voi medesimi you your
selves: Essi or loro medesimi, they them selves. So may you say, Essa medesima,
she her self, esse medesime they them selves.

Then have we other Nounes, called Possessiues, or else derived, and these are
they: Mio, mjei, mia, mie, mine, Tuo, tvoi, tua, tue, thine, Suo, svoi, sua,
sue: his: nostro, nostra: our: nostri, nostre, ours: of the Plural: Vostro,
vostra: your: Vostri, vostre: yours: the which are of each Gender, and of each
number, as Il mio libro: my book: i mjei parenti: my parents: mia madre: my
mother: mie sorelle: my sisters: il tuo ventre: thy belly: i tvoi libri: thy
books: tua mente: thy mind, or memory: tue scarpe: thy shoes. etc.

Then have we Cui, altrui, loro, these three Pronounes are always of the
second Case, as well in the one Gender, as in the other, and they are of a
possessing nature, Il cui merito: whose merit: la cui virtu: whose virtue: i
cui cos^umi: whose customs: and il lor pensjere: their thought: la loro mente:
their mind. etc. and l' altrui valor: others valor: l' altrui modestia:
others modesty: gli altrui tefori: others treasures. etc.

Then are there these other little parcels, but they are but seeldome used, if
they be, they are used of country men, and among the commons, and are joined
to other names, as Mo, ma, to, ta, so, derived from Mio, mia, tuo, tua, suo:
they may be called bastard Pronounes: them I pass over, because I would not
wish any to use them. etc.

Now will we (God willing) speak somewhat of the Participles.

I beseech you do so, if you be not weery.

Of the Participles.
THe last part of Speech, that through Cases does vary, is the Participle,
of the which I mind not to speak much, because Scipio Lentulo has done it
very well. For the more I go about to make it agree with the Latin, the
further I enter into confusion, and into a thing more vain, then necessary:
For the Italian may almost altogether be without them: and in many places it
does abhor them, as a man would say, Beato il temente Iddio, e credente a la
sua parola, e sperante ne le sue promise: Blessed is the fearer of God, and
believer of his words, and hoper in his promises.

Such manner of speech is abominable in our speech, for in stead of it, we use
to say, Beato chi teme Iddio, e creed a la sua parola, e spera ne le sue
promise: that is, Blessed is he that fears God, and believes his word, and
hops in his promises. And thus much is for the present time.

But for the time passed, the number is very great, and for the Future time, it
is but once used, and that is taken from the Latin, and yet may the Italian
language (as) well express her meanyngs, conceits, and thoughts, by Gerundes,
as the Latin with her Participles: the which sometimes are Gerundes, and
sometimes Participles, as if I will speak of time present, I shall say: Io
correndo mi stanco: I runnyng, weery me self: for mente corro: whilst I
run. If of passed ryme, I shall say, A vend' io corso, son tutto sudato: I
having run, am al sweatty: for Poi che ho corso: or else, perch ho corso. If of
the Future time, I shall say, Dovend'io a correre: or else, Io che son per
correre: or else, Io che debbo correre: or else, Io che voglio correre: or else,
Io che ho a correre ^^ voglio spogliare: that is, I being to run: or else,
having to run, or I that am to run, Or, I that must run, or, I that have
to run, will put of my clothes. See how many we have for one, yea many more,
and this we have in the Italian Gerondes that the Latin have not, we know
the time and they do not, because there is certain voices that seem
participles and are not, and many that are not and yet seem to be, for to be
sure of that, you must note the time, for when they signify time they are
participles, and when they do not, they are not, and so I think this
sufficient concerning them. Until now have we talked of the elements, of the
parts of speech, mutable through cases, now will we come to those which are
mutable through time, the which are verbes.

Of the Verbes in general.
THe verb is that part of speech which only does vary through time, for
because the Verb is he which signifies the time, as it is manifestly known:
now amongst al these verbes, some be as it were subject to a kind of order,
and some not: those which be of many ancient Gramarians have been divided into
four companies, and of them called Conjunctions. The first of the which, are
those that end in are, with the accent on the last syllable save one, as,
amare, cantare, danzare; to love, to sing, to dance.

The second sort are those that end in, ere, and also with the accent on the
last save one, as, temere, to fear, vedere, to see, possedere, to possess.

The third sort are those that end also in, ere, but the accent on the last
vowell saving two, as, leggere, to read, scrivere, to write, ridere, to laugh.

The fourth and last does end in ire, with the accent on the last saving one,
as, Vdire, to hear, sentire, to feel, and sometimes to hear, partire, to
part, or depart, the verbes that are subject to such orders and rules are
infinite, but those that disagree from them are but a few. Of the first
conjugation this are they. Do, I give, Fo, I make Sto, I stand, Vo, I go.

Of the second, Cado, I fall, Debbo, shall I, Paio, I seem, Posso, I may, or
can, Soglio, I am wont, Tengo, I hold, Voglio, I will.

Those of the third are composed of Duco, as Produco, I produce, Riduco, I
reduce, Conduco, I conduct, Pongo, I set, Scjolgo, I loose, Tolgo, or Togli, I
take.

Those of the fourth, are Apro, I open, Dico, I say, Mvoio, I dye, Vengo, I
come, Ire, or Ire, To go.

With these are also joined those that end in Sco, as Nodrisco, I nourish,
Langvisco, I languish. And these are subject to no order, or rule.

Now if a man do stand in doubt of some Time, or Person, of which Verbes he
be, let him ouerrunne these few that are without order, and if he find it, he
shall be certified of the doubt, if not, he shall be sure that he is of the number
of those, that are subject to order. But because many times of our Verbes do
show them selves to agree with the Participle, and with the Verb Avere, to
have, or else with the Verb Essere, to be, it is to be known, that the Verbes
that are Transitiues, that is to say, that are named, or be incident in, or to
the thing that they treat of, then do they occupy the Verb Avere, as Io ho
letto molti libri: I have read many books. Io me l' avevo gvadagnato con l'
arm in mano: I had won it with weapons in hand, but those that touch the
party that speaks, they end with these small parcels, Mi, ti, ci, vi, si,
which do answer to Io, tu, noi, voi. And others do occupy and use the Verb
Essere, as Io mi son ralegrato del' tuo been, ben che tu ti fossi doluto del
mio: I have rejoiced of thy welfare, although thou have sorowed of mine. And
Noici siamo accostati a lui; e voi vi sete vergognate di sarlo: Who have
approached our selves to him; and you have been ashamed to do it. Qvando essi
si saran pentiti de^l mal sare; noi ci consolere^o: When as they will be
repented of evil doing; then will we comfort our selves. But Posso, and Voglio,
do serve to both those Verbes above mentioned.

Now because there are certain things, that appertain unto all the Verbes, or
most part of them al these may be conjoined with the Verb Avere, because he is
the first, but those that particularly appertain either to one Verb or an
other, or else more to one conjunction then to an other shall be set in their
special places.

Now the Verb being (as we have said alredie) he that signifies, shows,
and notes the time, I know not for what occasion he should more be divided in
Moodes, then in his natural times, I will first that my Verb be divided in
his times, which are, the time passed, the time present and the time to come:
and unto this I add the diverse or variable time, as more plainly I will show
you hereafter, and although that the time passed do exceed, it is necessary
for us to begin in time present, because he is the root and chief cause of
our Verbes and talk, and not in the passed as the Hebrews do, and then the
diversities that are in each of this times are divided into certain Chapters,
the which but of mean scholars will be comprehended. Let the others know,
that even as those in the first age did but break, and stamp the grain, and
thereof did make a kind of past, and sodde it, which we call, Sugol^, or
Polenta, and did eat it and thereon fed, and when the true making of bread was
found, they would not lightly be persuaded, but that their old fashion was
best, and not the new. Even so will those do that are accustomed to this, Dio
volesse che, or, Dio voglia che, Dio haresse voluto che, and conejosia che,
concjosia cosa che, concjofusse stato che; and other such I will not force all
men to learn my rules, but those that will. This know I by experience, that
those that thoroughly observe them; although they never have been in Italy,
shall both speak, read, and write better then those that observe them. But
now let us come to the Declensions, or will you have it Coniugations? and let
us begin with the Verb, Avere, as,

The present Definitive.

Aggio, ave, and ae, are most commonly used of Poets.

The second person does commonly end in, i, in all the Verbes, but sometimes
the letters do vary, as, Dolgo, I plain, Dvoli, thou plainest, Dico, I say,
di, thou say, Paio, I seem, pari, thou seem, Pongo, I set, poni, thou
set, Seggo, I sit, sjedi, thou sit, Tengo, I hold, tjeni, thou hold,
vengo, I come, vjeni, thou come, Voglio, I will, vvoi, thou wilst, colgo, I
gather, coi, or cogli, thou gatherest, Scjolgo, I loose, or unbind, scjogli,
or scjoi, thou loosest, or vnbyndest, Soglio, I am wont, svoi, and svoli, thou
art wont.

Then are there some that have, n, before, g, and in some the, g, goes before
the, n, as cingo, cigni, to gyrd, attingo, attigui, to attain, or to draw
unto, Dipingo, dipigni, to depaint, gjungo, gjugni, to overtake, mungo, mungni,
to melch, Spengo, spegni, to quench, upon necessity may we say, piango,
piangi, or piangni, to weep, pungo, pungi, and pugni, to prick, stringo,
stringi, and strigni, to gripe fast, vnge, ungi, or vgni, to anoint: but fingo,
has only fingi, to feign, of glorio, we say glorio, to glory, and soffio,
suffij, to blow, for the others have the, i, pure, and these, liqvido, that is
moist.

The third person in the first Conjugation does end in a, as canta, ama,
salta, he sings, loves, ^eapeth, and such others. All the others do end
in, e, as vede, ride, ode, he sees, laugheth, and hears, in Posso, we may
say, pvo, pvote, and pvoe, he can, but the two last are Poetical. We also say
often svol, val, and vvol; and also, pon, tien, vien, riman, and such others
may be of the second and third, both the one and the other. The two first
plurals of this present time, and likewise the first of the varying time, are a
like, but to make them seem somewhat unlike, some writers have changed the,
m, into, n. and to make them also to disagree from the third that have the
accent on the last saving two, we put the accent on the last save one, and say,
Noi abbiano, facciano, diciano, we have, do, and say, this voice may also upon
occasion in all Verbes leave the last vowel, the Verbes that end in, sco, have
not this first person, we say, pogniamo, t^gm^mo, and traggiamo.

The second plural because it does not vary a little, may be known from
the infinitive, changing the, r, into ^, as amare, amate, dovere, dovete,
leggere, leggete, sentire, sentite, facete, and dicete, are not to be used,
sete, you be, or are, is of the Verb essere, to be, of the which we will
speak hereafter.

The third in all Verbes may loose, and leave the last vowell, saying, aman,
veggon, leggon, di^on, they love, see, read and say, but in this Verb and all
others of (monosilaba) we double the, nn, and say, hanno, danno, fanno, sanno,
they have give do, or make, and know, as partly I have told you in the
letter, n, yea sometimes we cast away not only the vowell but the whole
syllable, and say, han, dan, fan, san, we also say, possono, ponno, and pon,
debbono, denno, and den.

The time that follows is that, which until now we have given to the
imperative, the which certainly has a certain regard, or respect more then
the others, (as they say) means how to speak, for it does not always
command, but it exhorts, forewarnes, grants, forbyddeth, prayseth, and
gesteth, and diverse other such effects, having always a certain regard to
whom he speaks, wherefore I call them Respectiues, rather then Imperatiues,
and set him as a Sise, and not as a Gender, and here I speak only of his
Present, for his Future is set in his own place.

Respective.

have ye abbja 3 have he 3 abbiano have they

This second person of the singular number, in the first Conjugation, does
end in a, and in the third, in i: nevertheless sometimes to this i, do we
add an a, and say, abbja, sappja, debbja, in stead of abbi, sappi, debbi, and
such others. But these words are most used of Poets. In certain other
Verbes, we cast away the vowel, and say, Vjen, tjen, for vjeni, tjeni, and
diverse others. And this is done as well when a vowel follows, as a consonant:
yea sometime we cast away the whole syllable, and say, co, ve, to, for cogli,
vedi, and togli.

In stead of this time we may use the Infinitive, with the Negative, and say,
Non ti scordar di me: Forget not thee self of me. Non far cosi: Do not so.
Non l aver' per male: Have it not in ill part.

The first plural may leave the last vowel, and so we may say, Debbjamo,
debbjate, debbjano: We owe, you owe, they owe. And so, Sappjamo, we know,
vogljamo, we will, and such others.

The third plural likewise may loose the last vowel, and the last vowel save
one, may be i, and a. But yet in all Verbes, the first Conjugation has not
always the i, as, amino, love they, cantino, sing they.

In the Verb Dare, and Stare, we say, Diano, and dieno give they: Stiano, and
stieno, stand they. We are also better to say, Odino, and legghino, then
Odano, and leggano And so let this suffice you for the present time.

Now let us come to the passed, whose use will seem naughty, to the
scrupulous, but yet will yield a contentation and profit to the wise.

Of time passed, and uncertain.

From the first, and third, when they do end in a, you may take away thev,
and say, Vedea, I saw, or he saw, leggea, I read, or he read, and such others.

So may you do in the first and third in the plural, but not in the first
Conjugation, although that Petrarca has said, facea, and fea: which may be
granted to other Poets also. Avia, Solia, and such like, can not be granted:
Vedavamo, correvano, dioceavamo, and leggeavamo, and such like, are altogether
to be abhorred. Well may we say, Fallav imo, we failed, or did fail, so it be
in the Verb Fallare^ and not fallire.

Also may you say, Aggradare, and aggradire, apparere, and apparire, colorare,
and colorire, empjere, and empire, impazzare, and impazzire, inanimare, and
inanimire, pentere, and pentire.

The two voices of the second plural person, now adays are much used. The
first and third Plurals, may leave the last vowel. Potjemo, venjemo, and such
others, are to be fled.

Of time Indefinitiue.

Some ancient Writers have used E^, but it is not to be observed: this Person
does vary more then any other, and is very necessary to be known. The first
Conjugation of it does end in ai, as Amai, I loved, Cantai, I sounge. The
second has diverse terminations, as Potei, I could, or might: Ebbi, I had:
Ta'cgvi, I held my peace: Godetti, I did enjoy: and diverse such others.

The third likewise has as many, as Battei, I stroke: credetti, I did believe:
conobbi, I knew: chiefi, I requested: ruppi, I brake.

The fourth does also vary, as Vdi, I heard: apersi, I opened: venni, I came,
etc. When this voice ends in i, saying in stead of Vsaimi, and deliberaimi:
usami, and deliberami. And so, Direlo, farelo, for Direilo, and fareilo.

The second singular person does end in sti, in all Verbes, as Avesti,
fosti, amasti, vedesti: thou had, thou wast, thou louedst, thou saw, etc.

The third person does end diversely, whereof we will speak more hereafter.

The first plural person ends in mmo, as Amammo, leggemmo, We loved, we read.
etc.

The second ends always in ste, as Godeste, patiste: You enjoyed, and
suffered. etc.

The third also has many differences, whereof we will speak in other places.
But yet one thing we will note, that they al may leave the last vowel, as Ebber
amaron, sentiron: They had, loved, and felt, and such others.

Sometime it may leave the last save one, as Ferno, they did. Sometimes it
loses the whole syllable, as legaro, they bound. etc.

Avevo, avuto. 1 I had had. avevamo, avuto. 1 We had had. avevi, avuto. 2 thou
had had. avevi, avuto. 2 ye had had. aveva, avuto. 3 he had had. avevano,
avuto. 3 they had had.

These two first Preterperfect Tenses, together with that that goes before,
do signify from the Latin only in voice, but yet they have a contrary
signification.

Of that which goes before, we have already spoken: but of these three here
set together, it is to be known, that the first is nearest to the Present
tense, the which does show a thing done presently. The second is somewhat
distant from it. The third shows a thing far of, and done long ago. And
this is the cause why I have set them under these Genders, Near hand, Distant,
Far of. By the which Genders or Chapters, a man may know their difference:
For Ho avuto, does agree with time present, as, Qvando ho mangiato, voglio
here: When I have eaten, I will drink. Ebbi avuto, does answer the
Indefinitiue, as Qvand' io l' ebbi veduto, disubito 'l conobbi: When as I had
seen him, straightwayes I knew him. And avevo veduto, does somewhat touch the
thing passed, as Io l' avevo ben veduto, mano 'l conoscevo: I had well seen
him, but I knew him not.

We may also apply it otherwise, but it will be too tedious for me to rehearse
it, and for you to hear it. But yet one thing mark by the way, that the
Participles that do agree with the Verb Avere, may be joined, and may serve
both Genders and Numbers, as well in things going before, as in things
following, as Io ho desiderato lo tue comodita: I have desired thy commodities.
Io ho desiderato la tua comodita: I have desired thy commodity. So may you
say, Io ho desiderati i tvoi comodi: and Io ho desiderat e le ^ve comodita. But
all the Participles joined with the Verb essere, do accord always with the
Nominative Ca^e, as we will speak more of that, in the same Verb.

Condizionato. Conditioned, or agreed upon.

Under the Gender called Vario, are things pertinent to these three Preteritiues.

Avvenire. To come. Definito.
NV. SIN SIN. NV NV. PLV PLV. NV Avero, avro, or else, aro. 1 I shall have
Averemo, avremo, or, aremo 1 we shall have. averai, avrai, or, avra'. 2 Thou
shall have. averete, avrete, or, arete. 2 you shall have. avera, avra, or, ara.
3 he shall have. averanno, avranno, or, ara^no. 3 they shall have.

Upon the last syllable of the first and third person of this Future, the
accent is always needful in all Verbes: and in the last save one we always use
the e, as Cantero, I shall sing: prendero, I shall take. But the Verbes of the
first Conjugation use the i, as Vdiro, sentiro, I shall hear, and feel. And
those of Monosillaba have the a, as Daro, faro, I shall give, and make.

There are many Futures, that are more used eclipped, then whole, as Avro,
sapro, for avero, sapero, I shall have, or know. Diraggio, and faraggio, are
not to be used, unless it be upon great necessity, in Rhyme, but Diro, and
faro, etc.

he shall have. avranno or aranno. 3 they shall have.

The third singular also of this Future, must have the accent upon the last
syllable, sometimes and often in the third plural it leaves the last syllable,
and we say, in steed of faranno, aranno, faran, diran, So is it in many Verbes.

This Future is different from the first, for the does show us a certain
time conditioned, and indefinitiue, as. Qvando io l'aro veduto lo credero: When
I shall have seen it, I shall believe it. Or else, S'egli l'ara trovate le dara:
If he shall have found it, he will give it thee.

Infinitive.
Dover avere, Esser to be or have to have. per avere, Aver, ad avere.

As for the voices of our fore passed Gramarians set under the Optatiue, and
Subiunctiue moodes, do not we see? And they also confess it, that they show
naught else but a conditioned speech. And that by some of them, one can not
know rightly what time one speaks of. Wherefore they seek to help them
with such like voices, as. Dio volesse che: Would to God that. Or else,
Concjosia cosa che: For as much And many such others, and this is worst of all,
that many times some thinking to speak of present, or passed time, speak of
the Future, as. Se tu vemissi a trovarmi io ti parlerei: that is, If thou
should, did, or would come and find me, I would speak to thee, is
not this a manifest Future, as they say, Wherefore and in consideration of
that, those voices that do not show a manifest time, are by me set under a
Gender called in Italian Vario, that is diverse, varible, or uncertain, the
which is added to the three times.

Variable, and conditioned.
NV. SIN. NV. SIN. NV. PLV. NV. PLV abbja, or aggia, 1 have I or let me have.

abbjate. 2 have ye abbja, or aggia. 3 have he or let him have abbjano, abbino,
or aggjano. 3 have, they or let the^ have

All these three singular persons commonly end in, i. in the first
coniugations, as. Io ami, tu ami, colui, ami. And in the fourth all three in,
a, as: Io senta, tu senta, colui senta. But in the second and third, they have
the first, and third person end in a, and the second in, i, as. Io vegga, tu
vegghi, colui vegga, to scriva, tu scrivi, colui scriva. Also some in the first
person do end in, i, and, a, as, Vogli, and voglia, mvoi, and mvoia, dichi,
and dica, also some do end in, a, all three persons though they be not of the
fourth, also some end in the Verb, So, I know, as, Io Sappja, tu sappja, and
sappi colui sappja. And some all three persons have both ends, as. Io faccja,
and facci, tu faccja and facci colui faccja and facci, of the first Plural, we
have spoke of it before in the definitive of the present. We may say, Vegnjamo,
or venghjamo, let us come, also, Vdiamo, udiate, and odiamo, odiate: Hear we,
or let us hear, and hate we, or let us hate, the one is of the Verb Odo, I
hear, the other of the Verb, Odio, I hate, in the third we find, Veggjano,
and Vegglino, diano, and Dieno, siano, and Sieno, this person may leave the
last vowell.

Pattovito, That is upon condition, or Bargain.
NV. SIN. NV. SIN. NV. PLV. NV. PLV avessi, or avesse, 1 had I, avessimo, 1 had

The first and third singular persons have their end in e, or else, i, but the,
i, is fittest for the first, and e, for the third, the other declensions are
Poetical, also, Venesse, fusse, perconsse, for Venisse, fosse, percosse,
facessi, and fessi, are as well of the second, as of the first.

In this second person may you add tu, and take away the last syllable, and
say, Avestu, fostu, and vedestu. In the third plural, we may easily leave the
last vowel, and say, Avesser, avessin, and such others.

The Latin has not this Time. Sometime we say, Potre, sare, for Potrei,
sarei, aand also for Potrebbe, sarebbe.

To the second person in this Time, we may add, and join unto it, tu, and
fay, Avestu, potrestu. Averieno, with the accent on the last syllable save one,
is not to be used.

Infinitivo. Infinitive Mood.
Avere. 1 To have.

This Time often leaves the vowel, and often becomes a Noune, and has the
Masculine Article, and is in all Cases, and agrees both with Nounes and Verbes,
and immutables, as Il fuggir de'l tempo: The fleeing of the time. Un batter
d'occhjo: an ineling of na eye. And many such others, the which to rehearse,
were but labor lost. For all books are full of such. We say sometimes,
Scjorre, and scjogliere, torre, and togliere. We say, Bear, porre, but not
Bevere, and ponere. And thus let this suffice you for this Verb.

Of the Verb Essere, To be.
THis Verb is very necessary to the Italian tongue: in so much as we have no
Verb Passive, nor can express the Passive voice, without the help of this
Verb: wherefore I think it good to decline it, and then to give over, hoping
that by these two Verbes, and by the Rules before given, they will suffice you.
For to make a long discourse, and to show the declinyng of them, it would
require a great volume, and (as I suppose) would trouble and weery the Reader.
For I know this of a certainty, that if twenty men do study the Italian
tongue, perhaps not one of them does learn the Grammar: and that is the cause
why they never attain unto the perfection of it. And again, there are so many
Verbes declined by Scipio Lentulo, and William Thomas, that I refer you to
them, still remembering my Rules, (as very necessary) and also (by the help of
God) I mean hereafter, and that shortly perhaps, to set out another book very
necessary for the learner of it, the which I mean to translate, as the best,
perfectest, and surest way that ever hitherto has been devised.

Wherefore I pray you to accept this by the way, in good part, and remember it
is given for good will. And if it please God that I do the other, I will show
things there more at large, and especially the Verbes: and other things to,
the which I hope will do you much good.

Surely sir, these rules which you have already given me, are very good, and by
them I hope to attain unto the perfection of it, with a little labor, yet
notwithstanding if you will give me any other rules, I shall think my self
much bounden unto you, not only I, but a great many more of my country men,
which do take great delight in the Italian tongue. Wherefore I pray you forget
not your promise but proceed even when most you please, and remember where you
left.

I remember it very well, I promised you to decline the Verb. Essere. To be,
the which is this.

Essere, To be, of the present time and definitive.

Essere. To be.

This Verb is also used among the principles, as Sidto, staca and Suto, suta,
to have been in the singular number, and Stati, ta^^, and Suti, suit, to have been, in the plural number.

To this Verb will we apply thee, Gerondes and these are they.

Of present time, Essendo, being. Of time past, Essendo stato, stata, stati,
state, Having bin. Of time to come, Dovendo esserre, avendo ad essere, and
essendo per essere, being or having to be,

As for the Verbes, that are subject to a rule, and are of the four
Coniugations, these are they that follow, but I will use brevity and refer
them to the courteous readers judgment.

Of the first Conjugation.
Amo, I love, ami, thou love, ama, he loves, amiamo, we love, amate, ye
love, amano, they love, amai, I loved, amasti, thou louedst, amo, he loved,
amammo, we loved, amaste, you loved, amarono, amorono, amaron, amar, they
loved, amare, to love, esserper amare, dover amare, aver ad amare, to be or
have to love. The Futures of the first Conjugation, that are more used clipped,
then whole, are these and such like, Andro, dimor^o merro, perro portro; that
is I shall go, stay, lead, pain, carry, you may also say, Andaro, dimorero,
menero, penero, portero, as partly we have talked of before.

Of the second Conjugation.
Veggo, or Veggio, I see, vedi, thou see, vede, he sees, vegglamo, vedemo,
we see, vedete, you see, vedono, or veggono, they see, vidi, I saw, vedesti,
thou saw, vide, he saw, vedemmo, we saw, vedeste, you saw, videro,
vider, they saw, vedere, to see, aver a vedere, esser per vedere, dover
vedere, to be, or have to see, the Futures that in this Conjugation are used
most times short, and eclipped then whole, and long, as we have said above in
the general rule, are these, as, avro, bero, cadro, dovro, parro, rimarro,
sapro, sosterro, terro, varro, vedro, that is I shall, Have, Drink, fall, owe,
seem, remain, know, sustain, hold, be worth, see, and certain others which
are not so much in use.

Of the third Conjugation.
Leggo, I read, leggi, thou read, leg, he reads, leggiamo, we read,
leggete, you read, leggono, they read, Lessi, I read, legesti, thou reddest,
less, he read, leggemmo, we red, legeste, you read, lessero, lessono, they
read, leggere, to read, aver, a leggere, dover, leggere, esser per leggere, to
be or have to read, even as this word lessi, goes, so go these that
follow, Afflissi, condussi, cossi, fessi, and fendei, fissi, That is I
afflicted, conducted, sodde, cleft, fixed, and their compou^des, Mossi, ressi,
scossi, scrissi, strussi, vissi that is, I moved, ruled, received, writ,
destroyed, lived, and all do dobble: wherefore? We have told you above in the
Consonant, s, there are also other of this declension, but they do not dobble,
for they have consonantes going before the, s, as these, Arsi, colsi, corsi,
dipinsi, giunsi, finsi, estinsi, mu^si, punsi, piansi, porsi, scorsi, scjolsi,
sparsi, spensi, strinsi, sursi, svelsi, tinsi, torsi, vinsi, volsi, morsi, I
burned, gathered, ran, painted, overcame, fained, extinguished, milchd,
prickd, wept, reachd, ouerranne, let loose, despersed, quenched, griped,
risoppe, vnrooted, dyed, made cooked, overcame, or vanquished, turned, byt.
Other there are of this declension, which do not dobble, and yet have not a
Consonant to go before, the which do as it were imitate the latin tongue and
these are they. Accesi, ascosi, chjesi, chjusi, confusi, corquisi, difesi,
divisi, misi, or posi, that is, I kindled, hid, requested, locked, consuted,
conquered, defended, divided, set, and the compounded of these are, Presi,
rasi, resi, risi, rosi, risposi, spesi, tesi, vccisi. I took, shaued, rendered,
laughed, freted, answered, spent, tended, kilde, or slew. They have also other
de^lensions, which are, Conobbi, cr^bbi, nacqvi, nocqvi, ruppi, roppi, and
rompet. And so Battei, concepei, credei, empjei, perdet, procedet, and
processi, rend^i, ricevei, vendei: I knew, did grow, was borne, annoyed broke,
struck, conceived, believed, filled, lost proceeded, rendered, received, sold.

This may also end in tti, and say, Ricevetti, resistetti, and such others.

In the third person we say, Perde, perse, or, perdette, and so forth he lost.

Of the fourth Conjugation.
ODo, I hear, odi, thou hear, ode, he hears, Vdjamo, we hear, Vdite, ye
hear, odono, they hear. Vdi, I heard, Vdisti, thou heardest, Vdi he heard,
Vdimmo, we heard, Vdiste, ye heard, Vdirono, udiron, udiro, udirno, udir, they
heard. Vdire, to hear, avere udito, to have heard, dover udire, avera udire,
esser per udire: To be, or have to hear.

Odiamo, odiate, are of the Verb Odiare, to hate, and not Vdire.

The Verbes that are clipped in this Conjugation, are Verro, morro, I will, or
shall come, dye. A few others there are, which are used most commonly of some
Poets, but they go very hard.

The Conjugation of the Verbes passiues.
Son' amato, sei amato, e amato, I am, thou art, he is loved, Siamo amati, sete
amati, sono amati, we are, you are, they are loved, essere amato, to be loved,
essere stato amato, to have bin loved.

Of the impersonals I will speak nothing, because they are not so much in use
of the common sort, but of the learned Poets, and I think but few Poets will
learn of me.

Of the Verbes which be not subject to rules.
Already we have told you that there are some Verbes that are content to be
subject to certain rules. Of those that are subject to such rules, somewhat we
have spoken of before. Now let us come to the others, that are not, and
somewhat do disagree from the other, and to the end they may be better known,
I have here set them to the four Coniugations: those of the first Conjugation,
are four, agreeing with the Monosilaba, and these are they Do, I give, so, I
make, or do, sto, I stand, vo, I go.

Of the first Conjugation.
DO, I give, Dai, thou geuest, da, he gives, diamo, we give, date, you give,
danno, dan', they give, Detti, djedi, djei, dje, I gave, desti, thou gave,
debt, djede, he gave, demmo, we gave, deste, you gave, djedero, dettono,
dettero, d^eder, djero, djer, they gave, Dare, to give.

Fo, and faccio, I make, or do, fai, thou make, or do, fa, face, fae, he
makes, or does, faccjamo, fate fanno, and san, we, you, they, make, or do.
Feci, fei, I made, or did, fa^ esti, or festi, thou madst, or did, fece, fe,
he made, or did, facemmo, femmo, we made, or did, faceste, feste, you made, or
did, fecero, fer^no, ferono, fero, fer, they made, or did, fare, to make, or
do.

Vo, and vado, I go, vai, thou go, va, vae, he goes, andjamo, we go and
ate, you go, vanno, van, they go, andai, I went, andasti, thou wentest, ando
he went, andammo, we went, andaste, you went, andarono, andorono, andaro,
andar, they went, andare, to go, dover andare, esser per andare, aver, ad
andare, to be, or have, to go.

S^o, I stand, stai, thou stand, sta, he stands, stiamo, state, stanno,
stan, we, you, they stand, stetti, I stood, stesti, thou stoodest, stette,
he stood, stemmo, steste, stettero, sterono, steron, stero, we, you, they
stood, stare, to stand, dover stare, esser per stare, aver astare, to be, or
have, to stand.

Of the second Conjugation*
THose that are subject to no rule of the second Conjugation, are these, or
such like, Cado, debbo, dolgo, paio, posso, so, soglio, tengo, voglio, I fall,
shall, or owe, complain, seem, can, know, am wont, hold, will.

Cado, or caggio, I fall, cadi, thou fallest, cade, he falls, cadjamo, or
caggjaemo, cadete, cadono, or caggiono, me, you, they fall, cadere, to fall.
etc. The Verb cascare, to fall is subject to a rule, all the rest not
specified go after the ordinary manner.

Of the third Conjugation.
THe Verbes in this third Conjugation, do differ somewhat from the other, for
they are so eclipped and whosoever looks not well to the^, may lightly err,
If I should declare them all, it would be to tedious. Wherefore I will but
show the variety of one only, for example and that shall be, Toglio, I take,
toglio, tolgo, I take, togli, toi, thou take, toglje, toll, toe, to, he
takes, togljamo, we take, togljete, ye take, togljmo, tolgono, they take,
togli, to, te, take thou, togljete, take ye, togljevo, I did take, toglj^vamo,
we did take, ^olsi, I took, togljesti, thou tookest, tolse, he took,
togljemmo, togljeste, toljero, we, ye, they took, ho tolto, I have taken,
^bbi tolto, I had taken, avessi tolto, had I taken avrei tolto, I should have
taken, aver tolto, to have taken, torro, I shall take, torrai, thou shall take,
t^rra, he shall take, torremo, torrete, torranno, we, ye, they shall take,
torre, tor, togliere, to take, esser per torre, aver a torre, dover torre, to
be, or have, to tak, in my next book you shall have this Verb set out at
large.

Of the fourth Conjugation.
IN the fourth Conjugation, without the Rules, are Ire, and ^ire, to go. There
are also certain others, which somewhat do vary, but in the Indefinitiues
only, as Apri^e, dire, morire, salire, venire, To open, say, dye, ascend,
come* and certain others, in which I will use brevity. And here is the end
of one of the two principal divisions of the speech (as I told you before) that
is to say, of the two mutable parts. Now let us come to the immutable.

Of the immutable part.
THe immutable, or indeclinable, (take which you like best) have been
heretofore of many learned men, divided into four chiefest parts, and
parcels, which be very necessary, and so behoueful, that the Italian tongue may
not in any wise be without them, because they are such an ornament and
beautifying of it: and these are they, the Aduerbes, the Conjunctions, the
Prepositions, and the Interiections: whose significations I will briefly show
unto you, and first we will begin with the Aduerbes.

Of the Conjunction.
Now even as we have declared the Aduerbes, and the meaning of them, now will
we come to the Conjunctions, and will do the like of them, hoping that by them
you shall do much good, for they are very necessary, if they be well noted.

Of the Prepositions, and Interiections.
THe Prepositions and Interiections, heretofore of some have been divided into
two parcels, and of other some they have not, but have gone together in one.
Now I considering of them, have thought it good, to let them both go
together, for so much as many words are both Prepositions, and Interiections.
And being separated, would as it were (to the unlearned) breed a kind of
confusion. Wherefore I pray you to accept them in good part, and weigh my good
will.

Of the Prepositions, and Interiections.
Prepositioni. Prepositions. In, as in cjelo, and in terra. In. etc. Ne, as nel
cjelo, and ne la terra. A. To. ad. Di. Of. de. Da. From, or of. Per. For, by,
or through. Dopp. After. Sopra. Upon. Di sopra. Above. Olire. Beyond, or
beside. Djetro. Behynd. Apresso. At, with, by, or after Apetto. In comparison.
Con. With, as withhym, or her. Con esso, or essa. Senza. Without, as without
him. Ver Toward. verso in verso. Intra Between, amongst or within. infra tra
fra. Sotto Under. di sotto. Contra Against. contro incontro. Accanto Near, by
the side. allato presso. Rimpetto Oueragainst. per mezzo dirimpetto. Fin Until,
or unto. fino sino infino insino. Avan^i Before, or in presence Dinanzi Inanzi

Now God willing will we draw to an end, and make a conclusion of this
little work, for I am sure, you are weerie to hear so long a process.

Not so sir but I pray you proceed.

You are to note that in this language we have certain little parcels as it
were prepositions, which never go alone, but are always joined to other
words, and specially to Verbes. And these be they and other such like, as.

Dis in Dissare To undo Di in Dissido I mistrust. Es in Esalto I exalt. Mis in
Miscredere To misbeleeue. Pos in Pospongo I set against. Ra in Raccoglio I do
gather. Re in Respiro I sigh again. Ri in Ripiglio I do take again. Tra in
Trametto I do put between. Fra in Frastaglio I do hacke or choppe. Tras in
Trasporto I do transport Inter in Interompo I do interrupt.

Then are there other such little parcels which are used often, marieas, it
were, for an ornament of the speech then for necessity, and they be these,

Egli, as, Egli non e ancor gran tempo passato,

It is not yet long time passed.

Ei, as, Ei no'l sapr a may persona,

No person shall ever know it.

Ne, as, Io me ne parti e me n'andai,

I parted and went away.

E, as, E mi pare vna gran cosa, che,

Me thinks it is a great thing, that.

Ben, as, Ben mi pareva di strano, che,

It seemed strange unto me, that.

Pur, as, Sta pur avedere,

Say, stay, and see, or behold.

There rests but one thing to show, and that is to show the strange,
diverse, and sundry meaninges of the word, che, and the conioyning of it, with
other words, of the which I have been requested diverse times of sundry my
friends, and also I think it a good thing, and necessary to all men.
Although somewhat we have spoken of it above yet we came nothing near it.
There is no voice so much, and so often used in the Italian tongue, as is, che,
and that is because it has so many significations, for sometimes it is
Relative, and other whilst not, and accords with all, Cases Numbers, and
Genders, and when it has an Article it has the nature of the Latin Neuter as
we have said above in the Pronounes. Sometimes, it serves as a beginning of a
naration, after the preface as thus, Dico adunqve: che, Wherefore I say that,
che, sometimes serves as it were for a forewarning, as, T^iprego, che'l tuo
nemieo del mto mal non rida. I pray thee, that they enemy laugh not at my
evil, sometimes it does as it were answer unto that, that is spoken before
and has the meaning of perch, as.

L'anima, ch'e sol da Dio e satta gentile,

Che gia d' altrui non puo venir tal gratia.

My soul that only of God is made gentle,

For that such grace can come from no other.

Sometimes it shows as it were what may happen, as thus,

Non fare; che non te ne avenga qvalche male.

Do not; to thend there happen not some mischance to thee.

Sometimes it does as it were, show the matter and substance, of that, that
we talk of, as thus,

Qvesta novella djede tanto che ridere,

This news gave cause of such laughter,

Sometimes it shows the meaning of, il medesimo, but after another manner, as
thus,

Io voglio, che tu sappi, che io ti porto grandissimo amore,

I will, that thou know, that I bear thee very great love.

Sometimes it is dobbled and signifies, cjas cuna cosa che, any thing that,
and we say, che, che.

Sometimes it is set as a beginning of every chapter or else diverse parcels, as
thus,

Che'l diamante e pju duro di tutte l'ltre pjetre,

That the Diomond, is harder then all other stones, or else thus,

Che'l cjelo e pju duro che 'l diamente,

That the heaven is harder then a Diomond.

Sometimes it makes as it were a comparison, as thus,

Pju duro che'l Diamante.

More harder then Diomond, or else,

Alessandro fu pju fortunato, che alcun' altro signore del mondo,

Alexander was more fortunate, then any other Lord in the world.

Again it is dobbled and joined to many other words and sayings, as partly
we have showed before and partly will show here, and then make an end.

And thus I end beseeching all courteous gentlemen to accept my good will, and
still remember it is not my profession, that which I have done, I have done
for good will, and did it only to pleasure a private friend of mine, not
thinking it should have come to light, for if I had I would have taken
greater heed, if it be well accepted I ask no more, if it be not; blame not
me, but blame my friend that was the cause thou see it. If I can see or
perceive by any means thou do pleasure in it, I promise thee a better, if
not, here I end for ever. If perchance thou find among the Aduerbes,
Conjunctions, Prepositions, and Interiections any word twise^, consider the
sundrye^ meaninges of them, for as they have diverse natures so diversely they
are placed. And thus I; commit thee to the Almighty, who prosper and keep
thee, and me to his will, and pleasure, and send us grace to serve him. From his
lodging in Woster place, thine to his power. I. F.

Patisco il male, sperando il been:
Sperando vivo, vivendo mvoro.

m' anderia invilupando in un simile Laberinto, che senza grand^ss ma fatica,

fareiun gran volume. Per tanto Iol scjo tal carico a ^^uio pju dotta di
me^njenie di manco il megljo che io so, and pjubre vemeute cho io posso, vi
daro qvi certe Regole altutto danon esser gittate v^a^* Sperando che tutta via
accetterete la ^^a bona volonia sempre aricordando vi che non e la mia
professione essendo io povero Artefice, e del tutto ignorante de similcose.

Qvesta Lingva Ingle se a dirla scjetta, credo che sia la pju confusa Langva de
tutte le altre, percjo che ella e derivata da molte altre ungve, e ogni di va
togljendo delle parole in presto: Molte ne piglia da la Italiana, pju da la
Todesca, assai pju da la Franzese, and infinite da la Latina, and anche taluna
da la Greca. Ma si amo come si voglia, il tutto rimetto al cortese Lettore, ma
qvesto tengo per fermo, che la-vera, and antica Lingva Inglese, e tutta dermata
de la Monosilaba; perch tutte qvelle paro^e che ha^no pjudi una silaba, sono
qvasi tutte derivate da altre Lingve: perch l'antica Lingva Inglese inanzi ch
ella fosse cosi confusa, e mescolata come e, poche parole haveres^i tri vate di
